Country: Bangladesh                                                     Project Title: Export Diversification Project
Proiect Descrintion
1. Project development objectives and performance indicators
(1) The Project's main goal is to accelerate Bangladesh's integration into the world economy by expanding net
export production value through product and market diversification, including increased value added. The primary
approach to this purpose is promotion of private market solutions to exporters' skill and information needs.
(2) Important subsidiary goals are to improve the business environment for industry and commerce generally -- and
thus help create employment and GDP growth - through reforms in trade policy, Customs administration, and
elsewhere to reduce costs now imposed by policy, procedural and infrastructural impediments. The Customs reforms
are particularly important, as the Project initiates a serious attack oii the corruption and inefficiency in revenue
collection which now consititutes a major business impediment.
(3) The extent of achievement of the overall goal by the end of the Project period (mid-2002) will be measured in
terms of increases in exports' share of GDP (which itself should grow faster as a result), increases in net value added
in export production, and declines in product/market concentration indices, compared with base FY1998/99. Larger
gains in each case should accrue beyond the Project period (from FY 2002/03 onwards), as the Project is intended to
lead exporters up a entrepreneurial skill learning curve and to create a broader export base on which the more
skilled entrepreneurs can build. Progress by mid-2002 will be measured qualitatively.
(4) Progress on achieving the broader business environment and public administration goals will be measured in
terms of improvements in service delivery in selected Customs operations, either the resumption of trade
liberalization or enhanced public and official readiness for such reform, and sustainable enhancement of capacity in
several trade policy and export service agencies        (See Annexes I and IA for speciJfic achievement indicators)
2. Project components                                                  (See Annex 2for detailed descriptions)
The Project is divided into two broad components, one providing product and market development support to private
firms, and one dealing with trade management capacity building mainly in the public sector. Each comprises self-
contained but coordinated subprojects to address particular institutional needs.
(I) The product and market development support component emphasizes entrepreneur capacity building and
promoting markets in export support services:
-  Its main subproject consists of administration and advisory services for the operation of a matching grant facility
for exporting firms, groups of such firms, and service providers (MGF)
-  Funding for the facility is also part of the Project and its supporting IDA Credit.
*  Smaller subprojects to strengthen selected public, private and public/private support service providers, not
amenable to matching grants, are expected to be developed and implemented during the Project period
(2) The trade management capacity building component contains three subprojects focussed on key aspects of the
policy and institutional environment for export and other business
*  One constitutes the first phase of Customs modernization, including more rapid clearance for exports and their
imported inputs, better bonded warehousing and duty drawback schemes, automation through ASYCUDA++ or
an equivalent system and through other management information systems, the piloting of post-event audit, risk-
management and other modem methods, taxpayer education, and extensive staff training (CM-i). As well as
initiating Customs reform, this subproject serves as the first significant step in a more far-reaching program to
modernize the whole revenue administration which will be supported in future projects.
*  Another subproject will strengthen capacity in the Bangladesh Tariff Commission for trade data and protection
policy analysis, and in both BTC and the Ministry of Commerce for international and regional trade cooperation
(PATC), The first of these aspects will provide a strong technical basis for the expected resumption of trade
liberalization, while the second will enhance Bangladesh's participation in cooperation arrangements.
*  A coordination and development unit will undertake analysis of export constraints and institutional change needs,
formulate new subprojects to support exporters, and facilitate and monitor the other Project activities (PCU).

3. Benefits and target population
(1) The expected benefits of the Project will accrue initially to current and potential owners and workers of export
production units which are able to take advantage of both an improved operating environment and market-based
support for their development of new products and markets. Most fimns receiving such support will be small and
medium sized enterprises. Many export industry employees are female Since some of the improvements in public
management capacity are designed to facilitate backward links, the beneficiaries should include rural workers and
othier producers of inputs and intermediates who now have limited, if any access to export markets.
(2) The expected success of the initial beneficiary firms should encourage others to take up the export challenge,
leading in the medium term to significant increases in the quantity and range of exports and consequently in
enmployment, the balance of payments, and the economy's growth rate
(3) Further indirect benefits will accrue to consumers and taxpayers respectively if, as intended, the strengthening of
BTC analysis and formal reviews facilitate further tariff reform, and the streamlining of export-related Customs
operations paves the way for more far-reaching modernization of the revenue administration.
4. Institutional and implementation arrangements
(a) Implementation period
Commencing in June, 1999 with substantive work concluding 36 months later . A further 12 weeks for impact
analysis and external audit of the project will occur before the closing date of December 31, 2002. The period may be
extended by six months depending on the disbursement rate of the MGF. Three-year contracts have been approved for
four consulting firms which will mobilize their teams i-mnediately upon Credit effectiveness.
(b) Executing agencies
(1) Ministry of Commerce for overall project matters including the Coordination and Development Unit, the MGF,
and some of the new subprojects to be developed during the Project. (2) Bangladesh Tariff Commission in
cooperation with that Ministry for the PATC subproject. (3) National Board of Revenue for the CM-I subproject.
(4) Other agencies may execute some of the new subprojects (e g., Fisheries, Standards, Quota management).

(c) Project coordination                                                            (see chart on Project Structure)
(1) The Project Coordination and Development Unit (PCU) will be the operating arm of the Technical Comrnittee
(see below) so its facilitation and monitoring function will be ensured through collaboration among the project
directors and advisory teams of the several subprojects (including new ones under the PMDS component which it will
help develop). It will be staffed bv three full-time advisors (an internationallv recruited Chief Technical Advisor, a
Deputy CTA and a Financial Management Specialist), a procurement specialist. a variety of international and local
short-term experts, and seconded staff of the Federation of Chambers of Commerce and Industry Apart from their
project coordination tasks, the PCU advisors and experts will be engaged in substantive work on export constraints
and institutional reorganization.
(2) Each subproject also will have its own internal coordination arrangements. with functions shared between the
relevant project director and the contracted firm represented by a senior technical advisor (or equivalent); these will
be accountable respectively for the official facilitation and control of project activities, and for the flow of expert
inputs including the quality of their products and the effective transfer of skills to local staff. In the case of the
Customs subproject, the PD and the senior advisor will be coordinating several complementary activities: the
contracted team of advisors and trainers, the involvement of WCO for further training; the involvement of UNCTAD
in the installation of ASYCUDA++ (or of an alternative supplier and system) and related training; and the
procurement of equipment. Particular care will be taken to ensure that equipment and training are delivered
appropriately to facilitate and complement process and organizational reform.
(3) Project tasks will be closely coordinated with complementary activities outside the Project itself, including: (a)
the parallel Trade Policy Capacity Building Project and proposed WTO help in the case of PATC, and (b) the
Customs subproject's relationship with (i) UK support for VAT reform over the same three-year period, and (ii)
proposed support for parallel reforms in the income tax service for which another IDA operation may be requested.

(d) Project oversight and policy guidance                            (see chart on Project Structure)
(1) The Steering Committee is chaired by the Secretary of Commerce with the Chairman of NBR and the President
of the Federation as other core members, joined by heads of participating agencies. NBR Members for Customs and
for Administration and Reform, Chairman of BTC, Director-General of Duty Exemption and Drawback Office, and
Vice-Chairman of Export Promotion Bureau. Participation may be invited where relevant from other Ministry heads
- e.g., Secretaries of Finance, Economic Relations, and Planning, and Governor of Bangladesh Bank.
(2) The SC will be supported by a Technical Committee chaired by the Project Director for Coordination
(Additional Secretary of Commerce) and comprising the other PDs (senior officials of NBR and BTC) and the senior
technical advisors or equivalent (i e the leaders of the consulting teams for the various subprojects).
(3) It will be supported also by an Audit Committee - see (e) below
(4) The matching grant facility will be supervised by a separate MGF Board comprising an independent chairman,
representatives of the FBCCI and participating industry associations (three selected annually from among the
associations), and senior officials of Commerce and Finance The chief of the MGF management unit (as secretary)
and the CTA from the PCU will participate as non-voting members. Policy guidance is provided through the
Statement ofPolicies and Operating Procedures for the Matching Grant Facility (see An7tex 2A).
(e) Accounting, financial reporting and auditing arrangements                           (See Anniex 6)
(1) The PCU will maintain a computerized financial management, accounting, disbursement and reporting system for
the Project and its subprojects in accordance with generally accepted accounting principles and practices and using
the World Bank Group's "COSTAB" and "LACI" formats. To facilitate this, the PCU staff will include a full-time
Financial Management Specialist who will report to the PD for Coordination through the CTA, and will supervise an
Accountant, the part-time Procurement Specialist, and the Unit's support staff. They will prepare and operate under
quarterly and annual budgets and an accounts manual, with advice from IDA's resident PDAT staff as needed to
ensure consistency with IDA requirements under OP/BP 10.02.
(2) A Special Account will be opened either in the central bank or in a designated commercial bank. Disbursements
will be made by the PCU or NBR project officials directly from the Special Account
(3) The accounts and records to be maintained will be capable of disclosing a true and fair view of the financial
situation and of facilitating progress monitoring for each subproject and for the Project as a whole. While accounts
will be kept on a cash basis (following GOB practice), outstanding expenditures will be accounted for review at least
annually Activity-based subsidiary records will be generated and kept current on a daily basis by subproject staff,
with guidance from the FMS and technical assistance as necessary from the Accountant and other PCU staff.
(4) Formal reporting will include detailed quarterly and annual statements in standard format prepared for review
and clearance by the Technical Committee, with summaries for the Steering Committee and IDA (and for the MGF
Board with respect to that subproject).
(5) Annual financial statements will be audited by an independent audit firn acceptable to IDA, and also by the
Office of the Comptroller and Auditor-General (C&AG) to ensure compliance with GOB rules and regulations.
Internal controls will include a separate Audit Committee advising the Steering Committee, proper segregation of
functional responsibilities, joint-signing of checks after clearance by the FMS, verification of procurement proposals,
monthly reconciliation of bank accounts, and regular detailed reporting
(Further details andfonnats are provided in the Borrower's Project Implententalion Plan (PIP) and in Annex 6. section B of this PAD)
(G) Monitoring and evaluation arrangements
(1) The PCU will be primarily responsible for Project monitoring and evaluation of progress being made towards
achieving the development objectives. It will develop benchmarks based on the project design summary (see Annex I)
and to be agreed with IDA and GOB, and will track inputs, processes, outputs and impact of all Project activities
using occasional PCU surveys of users' satisfaction, IDA staffs Periodic Economic Updates, official export
statistics, criteria the PCU will develop to measure success in training and other capacity building activities, and its
own specification and measurement of impact indicators (e.g,, export/GDP and value added/export ratios, Gini

coefficients, process cost/time reductions for business, and familiarity with external markets and practices).
(2) On a routine basis, the PCU will collaborate with subproject personnel in monitoring the implementation
schedules using "MS PROJECT" charts to be annexed to the PIP, and proposing adjustments to them as needed to
maximize Project benefits. Such adjustments would be approved by the Technical Committee and - for significant
changes -- by the Steering Committee and IDA
(3) Complementing the responsibilities of the Project Directors, the four contracted consultancy finns will be
accountable for the timely delivery and quality of the inputs and outputs promised under their contracts with GOB.
Therefore the four lead firms' headquarters are expected to play key roles in monitoring the performance of their own
teams in the field to help achieve the Project's objectives
(4) Internal Bank research has shown that TA projects are more likely to succeed when they are supervised
intensively Accordingly, IDA's country and sector managements intend to allocate sufficient administrative
resources over the next three years to facilitate regular supervision missions. These will (a) review and discuss
progress revealed by PCU and subproject monitoring documentation, and (b) participate in workshops and other
events to help assess stakeholders' perceptions of the Project's implenientation.
(5) Finally, provision has been made in the Project budget for GOB/FBCCI/MGF Board to employ separate
consultants acceptable to IDA to undertake independent evaluations of impact, conducted both at the Project's mid-
tenn (around December 2000) and following the 36-month period (i.e in July/August 2002). These will include
client surveys, and will focus particularly on (a) the sustainability of the capacity building activities and (b) the
degree and nature of measurable diversification that should have already begun to result from the grant facility and
other product and market development support activities.

Project Rationale
5. CAS objectives supported by the project                    CAS docunment number 17453-BD
Date of latest CAS discussion: March 31, 1998
(1) The Project supports several substantive and procedural CAS goals First, in substance it aims to accelerate
economic growth by improving the environment for private sector development, addressing all of the export-
related actions stated in the Country Program for that purpose, viz.
...prepare and implement time-bound trade liberalization program, based on a review by the
Bangladesh Tariff Commission; address policy distortions and institutional constraints that lead to
anti-export bias; improve functioning of key public sector agencies that interface with the private
sector - National Board of Revenue, Export Promotion Bureau, Tariff Commission ...; strengthen
the capacity of the Ministry of Commerce to use WTO arrangements and (the) Multi-fiber
Arrangement to ensure export competitiveness and introduce required export/import policy
changes; enhance private sector participation in formulating investment and tax/tariff policies; and
design and implement a Matching Grant Facility to assist exporters with product and market
diversification. "
(2) Second, the Project reflects the emphasis which the assistance strategy places on the need to focus IDA's support
more sharply on appropriate process:
*  capacity building to create a stronger institutional basis for the adoption and sustained implementation of
necessary reforms;
*  small, relatively simple operations, enabling successive IDA support operations to be related more closely to
reform commitment and performance;
*  the use of more direct means than in the past to strengthen private sector institutions and promote innovative
behavior by enterprises; and
*  inter-agency partnerships to ensure vital donor coordination.
With regard to the critical goal of capacity building, the Project includes initiation of a process of tax administration



Project Appraisal Document                                                                 ' Page 10
Country: Bangladesh                                                     Project Title: Export Diversification Project
reform which may take about a decade to implement fully, so will require sustained support through future IDA
operations. The need for such long-term commitment to capacity building is a key theme of the CAS.
6. Main sector issues and Government strategy
(a) The trade and tax regime
Although increasing or even maintaining the rate of growth of the Bangladesh economy has to depend on export
production (and efficient import substitution), the trade regime retains a significant anti-export bias that has
limited export growth and, more important, limited diversification Trade policy has three key operational
dimensions in this export-oriented but still rather highly protected economy:
(1) The general import regime itself: Following reasonable progress on trade policy reform, especially during
1993-95, the process has stalled with the average tariff rate remaining around 20% and dispersion that continues
to give high effective protection to some domestic market production. The strong anti-export bias in the regime
arises from both directly through the taxation of imports and indirectly through its effect on the relative
profitability of domestic market production and through its exchange rate effect of lowering the Taka value of
export proceeds. This is exacerbated for some industries, especially textiles, by a few remaining quantitative
restrictions. GOB has been expected to resume progress on lowering and compressing tariffs and removing QRs,
and to announce this in the context of the PFP/ESAF process. That process too has stalled in recent years,
however, in part because too few policy makers have been convinced of the advantages of an export-friendly
trade regime over one which supports the status quo.
(2) Schemes aimed at insulating exporters from part of the bias: Even if progress is resumed on tariff reform,
the need for mechanisms to remove duties and tax on imported inputs -- and their impact on prices of domestic
inputs -- will remain important through the medium term. The most popular of the present mechanisms, special
bonded warehousing (SBW) and duty drawback (DD), work quite well for dedicated, direct exporters, but they
have two unfortunate biases against firms producing for bothl export and domestic markets (partial export), and
against domestic sourcing of materials and intermediates (indirect export). GOB has understood for some time
the need to remove these biases and make the mechanisms available to a wide range of partial and indirect
exporters, but has feared the possible expansion of cheating under such a liberal policy so has been unable to
implement in the absence of more sophisticated monitoring and rate computation capabilities.
(3) Official trade cooperation. Bangladesh has always been a member of General Agreement on Tariffs and
Trade, but like other former colonies it avoided the informational and tariff binding rigors of accession now being
faced by, for example, the Eastern European and Central Asian economies in transition. Instead of trading
concessions within the GATT framework -- now the World Trade Organization -- it has liberalized its trade
regime unilaterally and participated in a few modest regional arrangements, notably the Bangkok Agreement.
Over the past few years it has been a leading proponent of the emerging South Asian Preferential Trade
Arrangement (SAPTA), but this has yet to result in significant changes in the trade regime. The Uruguay Round
has introduced the revised GATT, enhanced focus on customs valuation and product standards, accorded
automatic applicability to parallel agreements on agriculture, services (GATS), intellectual property (TRIPs),
government procurement (GPA) and trade-related investment measures (TRIMs), and -- perhaps most
significantly for Bangladesh - begun the phase-out of the Multi-fiber Agreement (MFA) Thus the need for
Bangladesh to play a more active role in the WTO has become more evident. At the same time, the economy may
benefit from rapid progress on regional and subregional trade cooperation. If full advantage is to be taken of its
membership of WTO, SAPTA, etc. to facilitate the growth and diversification of exports, an important
institutional issue to be addressed is the need to strengthen official capability in technical aspects of the
negotiation, compliance with, and enforcement of cooperation arrangements. Another key aspect is the need to
develop a balanced national approach to the rights of WTO members with respect to safeguards against
"troublesome imports" - to avoid a situation in which protection seeking vested interests may be encouraged to
find new legal ways of undoing the progress that has been (and hopefully will be) made on trade liberalization



Project Appralsal Document                                                                       Page 11
Country: Bangladesh                                                        Project Title: Export Diversification Project
(h) Tax and Customs administration
An issue closely related to trade policy, which has been receiving increasing recognition as a major drag on
efficient business growth is the endemic inefficiency of revenue administration, especially Customs which
administers the trade instruments Correct trade and tax policies are necessary but not sufficient to provide an
appropriate business environment; equally if not more essential is their effective implementation. This is
especially true for export business, where the costs of inefficiency and corruption cannot be passed on to
consumers. Past efforts to improve revenue administration in Bangladesh have been limited largely to the partial
computerization of collections data, the issue of more transparent rate schedules, and an unsuccessful experiment
with a "voluntary" pre-sbipment inspection, green channel scheme for importers. Until recently, GOB has not
addressed the need for more thorough changes in the revenue services, notably the pervasive dysfunctionality of a
bureaucratic institution with archaic structure, legislation and procedures, perverse incentives, and poor or
inappropriate relationships with "clients"- revealed by all institutional analyses of NBR.' Now, however, faced
with the combination of private business's frustration with inefficient processes which yield slow clearance and
disputed assessments even when bribes are paid, and tax receipts well below the level which high rates should
yield, the Government has decided to embark on a far-reaching administrative reforn In a recent statement it.
"...affrms the...need to improve efficiency and reduce inequity and rent-seeking in the entire
taxation system for economic growth and poverty reduction; and.. .recognizes that to meet these
needs a comprehensive Revenue Administration Modernization Program (RAMP) must be
mounted quickly to bring about appropriate changes in the organization, procedures, institutional
culture and afl other relevant areas in the national tax administration."2
(c) Product and market dlevelopment
(1) Bangladesh has movedfrom one narrow export base to another. It was rescued from its former dependence
on jute exports mainly by the very rapid growth of ready-made garment and knitwear export production.
However, this again involves a narrow product range, mainly at the low end of the quality spectrum this time. It
is also dependent on imported materials and on access advantages under the MFA quota and GSP systems.
After garnents/knitwear/specialized textiles (64%), the main exports are jute goods, frozen shrimp, leather, gas-
based fertilizer, some other chemical products, raw jute, frozen fish, tea, leather goods, and toys (no other
product-group yielded above 0.5% of gross export sales in 1995/96). Although some of these are based on
domestic raw materials, exports of horticultural and other such primary products are in infancy. This applies
also to most non-textile manufactures and to services other than sending workers abroad. Significant, swift
diversification is essential to reduce the risks of the ncw dependence on garments, heightened by the phasing out
of the MFA quotas which now advantage Bangladesh
(2) Such diversification is proving difficult to achieve Major issues affecting all potential export activities,
whether manufacturing, primary products or services, include (i) Bangladeshi entrepreneurs' lack of experience in
operating in competitive export markets, (ii) few foreign investments which could teach such experience to local
firms, and (iii) inadequate shipping and handling arrangements, including Customs clearance procedures. Other
issues thwart particular industries, such as lack of HACCP to avoid poor sanitary conditions for food products
and lack of ISO-9000 to facilitate those and many other products' access to some markets.
(3) Official exportpromotion efforts and technical supportfacilities, needed to overcome such difficulties,
have been modest in their impact. Little progress has been made on product and market diversification,
backward linkages, or product quality enhancement. This is not surprising, since it is the common experience for
l Institutional analysis explaining the need for extensive organizational reform as well as substantial changes in process and
bureaucratic culture of NBR has been provided in: a November 1997 paper by an internationally respected Customs expert
on SBWs and Customs Process Reform (see section 2 of Annex 2D), two 1998 reports - by IMF Fiscal Affairs staff and by
an HIID consulting team organized for NBR by IDA; the comprehensive 1997 IDA publication Governmnent That Works,
and a recent paper by the former Country Director on the Bangladesh bureaucracy (see Annex 8 for references).
2 January 12, 1999 letter to IDA on behalf of the Minister of Finance (see Annex 8) A draft of the program concept paper
outlining the various types of change has been discussed with and peer-reviewed by IDA staff and external experts.



Project Appraisal Document                                                                       Page 12
Country: Bangladesh                                                        Project Title: Export Diversification Project
countries with immature private sectors and thus reliant on public sector agencies which have a bureaucratic
rather than commercial approach to those functions. Most notable in this regard is the Export Promotion Bureau
(a part of the civil service with rather nominal private representation in its governance), but it applies also to the
official scientific establishment which should be backstopping product '-sting and certification, and to the public
facilities for vocational training. As well as desiring to strengthen some of these institutions, GOB is now
prepared to support innovative approaches involving more private participation and responsibility. These could
include public co-financing for individual firms and associations' developmental activities which otherwise may
not occur due to the externalities involved in pioneerinlg new products and markets.
(d) Other procedural and infrastructural constraints
It is often -- correctly -- argued by opponents of rapid trade reform that its short-term costs are raised when other,
systemic constraints prevent new, more efficient activities developing to reap the expected benefits of reform:
expanded employment, profitability, foreign exchange and other sources of more rapid growth. Existing
procedural and some infrastructural obstacles would undermine export initiatives even if the above-mentioned
support services were adequate. Although many of these obstacles have been reduced in recent years, costs in
time and money remain far above international norms for many physical and financial aspects of the movement of
goods through, from, and to ports and markets. Basic causes include outdated, inefficient and often corrupt
bureaucratic practices, weak information systems that fail to anticipate effective demand for supporting
infrastructure, and limited funds for public investment GOB has created some mechanisms for hearing and
reacting to exporters' concerns, but both Goverunent and the private sector still lack comprehensive, analytical
capacity to prioritize problems, quantify costs of inaction, propose feasible solutions, and implement them.
7. Sector issues to be addressed by the Project and strategic choices
To promote export development and especially diversification, the Project will address key aspects of each of the
export and other trade sector issues noted above. Perhaps most important, it will advance largely private initiatives
to serve potential exporters' immediate needs for support in developing a competitive export culture and specific
skills and experience in tackling new markets with acceptable products. Such needs include firm-, group- and sector-
level activities to improve product quality and quality control, adapt products to market needs, identify markets, and
upgrade technologies and skills for these purposes. With respect to public capacity building, the Project will focus
on technical and consultative aspects of: (i) needed improvements to expand coverage of the SBW and DD schemes
and to speed clearance, and the initiation of more general Customs and other tax modernization to tackle corruption
and inefficiency; (ii) the stalled process of unilateral liberalization, to make the case and build internal commitment
for further reform, combined with multilateral arrangements to expand trade opportunities, and (iii) identifying,
analyzing and overcoming other procedural and infrastructural constraints on new exporters More specifically.
(a) Private product and market development initiatives
(I) A strategic choice has been made to focus most of the Project's financial support for PMD on private
initiatives to develop and utilize support services and to "learn to export". The need for greater private
involvement in export promotion and technical support services would be addressed most strongly through a
Matching Grant Facility (MGF), an instrument which many developed countries and several Bank clients facing
problems similar to Bangladesh have found very cost-effective (see Annex 2C). Most of the grants are expected
to match expenditures by individual firms, both export producers and export service providers, while others may
be arranged to cofinance association-sponsored or other group activities which firmns may elect to execute
collectively in their subsectors. The key aim is to create markets in the supply and use of export support services.
(2) A second strategic choice has been made that the criteria for both types of grant would sharply reflect the
goal of new export product and market development (rather than the broad enterprise development approach used
in some other countries' MGF schemes). Uses of the grant funds are expected to include (but not be limited to)
innovative approaches to certification, standards and testing services, and enterprise training focussed on export
needs, as well as more direct teclnical assistance to help firms with product adaptation and market identification.



Project Appraisal Document                                                                    Page 13
Country: Bangladesh                                                     Project Title: Export Diversification Project
(3) A third pair of choices - aimed at minimizing the risk of politicization and abuse -- is for the MGF to be operated
by a Board on which private industry associations will have majority representation (rather than by a public agency),
and for that board to be enabled and encouraged to delegate most grant approval authority to the professional team
that has been contracted to provide advisory services to applicants and monitor results. Risk will be further reduced
by strict monitoring and impact criteria in evaluation of the Board and the managers' performance.
(b) Public backstopping for product and market development
In line with the above private sector focus, IDA and (only somewhat reluctantly) GOB have avoided insisting on
significant investment in strengthening public institutions for export promotion and support at this time. To
complement the private initiatives, however, it may be necessary for some public activities to be strengthened,
perhaps in areas such as quota management, trade intelligence, accreditation of private testers, and identifying
necessary policy changes or investments The Project may appropriately channel selective assistance in such
areas, preferably with private participation. First, however, it is essential to determine that the public institutions
concerned are really needed, prepared to function efficiently, and client-oriented. This may require reorganization
in some cases, so the Project Coordination and Development Unit is charged with institutional analysis to support
reform where necessary prior to further subproject development. The private activities noted above will be
coordinated appropriately with such subprojects and with assistance which other agencies may be rendering to
public entities (e.g. the International Trade Centre's traditional help to EPB).
(c) Duty and tax reliefprocedures
The Project would improve monitoring to make the SBW and Drawback schemes for relieving duty on imported
inputs less open to abuse -- essential for giving effect to GOB's willingness to remove their present biases
against domestic sourcing and partial export and for abandoning the present "cash compensation" scheme A
major issue addressed during preparation is whether investment in better relief schlemes can yield its expected
benefit without more fundamental changes in Customs administration culture. A strong consensus emerged that
real improvement in subsidiary schemes can be achieved best in the context of overall Customs reform (below).
(d) More comprelhensive revenue administration
(1) Meanwhile, as noted, concerns with the drag of inefficiency and corruption throughout the tax system -
impeding efficient business and limiting revenue receipts -- have increasingly been leading the authorities towards
acceptance of the need for a comprehensive revenue administrative modernization program (RAMP) that will
address these concerns by removing the drag inherent in transaction-based supervision. The Government is
continuing to refine and operationalize the RAMP concept, which it intends to begin implementing through the
Customs reform to be supported by the Project. It has decided that the first phase of this reform (as well as
addressing the above SBW/DD improvements and short-term actions to accelerate clearance of exports and
export-related imports) will pilot modern approaches to tax administration including self-assessment, post-event
audit, truly random inspection, and risk management A key objective of these techniques is to reduce the
opportunity for rent-seeking behavior by importers and officials. They will be complemented by strengthening of
other anti-corruption methods such as improved internal and external audit, enhanced vigilance and penalties,
performance-based bonuses, and taxpayer education and information saervices Some of the changes will require
new legislation, which the Government plans to introduce by end FY2000/0 1. Consideration will be given also to
more basic organizational change to enable better incentives for honest, professional conduct by Customs and
other tax staff. No decision has been made on how to deal with the incentives issue, although converting NBR
into a semi-autonomous Authority has been discussed tentatively in this regard. Lessons learned in the pilot
activities will be extended not only throughout Customs but to other tax branches as appropriate, either during or
following the project period as appropriate.
(2) The above approaches will be underpinned with a more advanced system of Customs data management
(such as ASYCUDA++ or TIMS) and other information technology to help improve conmmunication and
reporting in NBR, as well as with electronic detection equipment Although NBR's desire for IDA assistance
initially was focussed on these technology aspects, the present management fully comprehends that process and



Project Appraisal Document                                                                   Page 14
Country: Bangladesh                                                     Project Title: Export Diversification Project
structural changes are necessary to derive the benefits of automation. It recognizes that in "soft states" like
Bangladesh automation must be properly harnessed to yield its potential in (i) reducing contact between taxpayers
and officials, the latters' discretion, and hence opportunities for rent-seeking, and (ii) facilitating truly random
checks and proper audit and monitoring to deter and detect such behavior. Thus automation will be fully
integrated with process and cultural changes to be initiated through the subproject. The integration will be helped
by having a single consulting team to provide technical assistance in all the reforms and automation activities.
And it will be encouraged further by IDA authorizing some of the procurement only after a first batch of process
changes have been implemented in 1999
(3) In addition to IDA financing of the expert team and equipment, the World Customs Organization (WCO) has
agreed to participate in the subproject to facilitate human resource development and culture change, and
UNCTAD would be associated with the ASYCUDA installation if that system were to be selected.
(4) Parallel technical assistance from the UK, IMF and IDA is helping the NBR to launch complementary reforms in
the VAT and income tax administration. Later phases of all processes would be assisted by further IDA operations
to support more fundamental aspects of the RAMP if GOB remains committed to its full execution.
(e) The trade policy regime
If the currently delayed PFP/ESAF process is resumed to the point of securing GOB commitment to further trade
policy reform, the Project would complement that process by strengthening the Tariff Commission staff's analytical
capability and hearings process through advice and training. On the other hand, if GOB is reluctant to make an
immediate commitment to accelerate liberalization, the strengthening of trade policy analytical capability is designed
to yield opportunities for that commitment to be made and for implementation to begin during the Project, i.e. in the
FY200 1 and FY2002 Budgets Such opportunities will include formal joint GOB/IDA/IMF annual reviews of BTC
findings on the degree and effects of anti-export bias in the tariff and QR structure In addition, the status and
organization of BTC will be examined as a priority PCU institutional review, with a view to making the organization
a more effective advocate for balance among the interests of exporters, consumers, and domestic market producers.
(/ Official trade cooperation
(1) The Government sees an urgent need for the Project to help build up official capacity for negotiating formal
cooperation arrangements (both global and regional), for monitoring and ensuring compliance with their obligations,
and for facilitating the private sector's exploitation of opportunities they provide for trade expansion. This capability
needs to be built within the public sector, and MOC has determined that the consequent problems of staff turnover
could be mitigated by giving the Tariff Commission an important role in backstopping negotiations and compliance
activities. Accordingly, it has prepared a subproject which combines this activity with the above-mentioned
strengthening of trade policy analysis Although housed in BTC, the subproject will also train Ministry staff who are
formally charged with servicing the Government's participation in trade treaty negotiation, interpretation and
enforcement. The issue of turnover that makes it difficult even for semi-autonomous BTC to retain trained staff will
be addressed in another of the PCU's key institutional review tasks.
(2) In addition to IDA, the World Trade Organization (WTO) will be associated with the subproject through parallel
financing from the United Kingdom DFID's closely associated Trade Policy Capacity Building Project.
(g) Procedural and infrastructural constraints
As an adjunct to the PMD support, the Project will contain an "export constraints identification and analysis"
capability to deal with procedural and infrastructural issues not covered by the MGF because they may affect
numerous export industries. A few identified solutions may then be addressed by new activities that would be
developed within the Project, while implementation on others would require separate operations by IDA or other
lenders/donors. It is expected that several current needs would be either dealt with -- or at least identified for action
by others -- during the Project Equally important for the longer term, the capability to identify and analyze export
constraints and find sound, practical solutions on a continuing basis would be institutionalized in a public/private
entity (perhaps a reinvented EPB with much greater private participation and service orientation) to address issues as
they emerge in a dynamic export environment. The PCU's work would form a partial model for such an entity.



Project Appraisal Document                                                                       Page 15
Country: Bangladesh                                                        Project Title: Export Diversification Project
8. Project alternatives considered and reasons for rejection
(1) An adjustment credit (ISAC-3) was considered but could not be justified by presumed foreign exchange costs
of continuing tariff reform nor by fiscal needs related to export promotion Current BOP difficulties are being
handled by emergency IMF/IDA operations related to floods which sharply interrupted export growth in 1998.
(2) Inclusion of the export-related activities in a more general technical assistance credit (TA-7, which is at an
earlier stage of preparation than this Project) was considered, but that would lack the cohesion of a focussed
export operation and would have delayed processing and approval while other subprojects were being developed.
(3) Comprehensive strengthening of the official Export Promotion Bureau early in the Project was considered,
but the Borrower has agreed that this should be deferred to a later stage if/when GOB is able to reorganize EPB
into a compact, responsive, commercially oriented unit and after trying the more private approach reflected in the
MGF. Modest assistance, some in collaboration with the WTO/UNCTAD International Trade Centre, can be
given in the meantime however, and an early candidate may be support for strengthening the EPB's capability in
the RMG quota management and monitoring system -- in appropriate cooperation with the private sector
(4) A direct follow-on to the Export Development Project which provided rediscount funds for trade finance was
considered earlier and rejected because export credit problems, though serious, are due to the weak condition of
banking rather than funds shortage; moreover the US$30 million ED Fund is still available to rediscount working
capital on a rolling basis, while IDA's term finance Private Sector Investment Credit closed recently and a new
non-bank credit operation may be approved shortly.
(5) It was intended early in appraisal of the Project that the Customs subproject would be jointly funded with the
Asian Development Bank's proposed Port Efficiency and Access Improvement Project, which would have included
the installation of ASYCUDA++ in major Customs houses. Delays in the ADB project, unrelated to its Customs
component, have led to the integration of that component into the IDA Project, which has the added advantages of
simplifying financial design and control, and ensuring that automation will serve, not replace, basic process reform.

10. Lessons learned and reflected in the Project design
(1) Avoid attaching too much institutional reform to adjustment lending, but rather support it through TA
instruments like this Project.
(2) Ensure continuity/sustainability by complementing short-term advisory/training activities with political efforts to
bring about institutional/cultural change in public agencies, notably through strong ownership by stakeholders (in this
case export producers).
(3) Build firm arrangements for coordinating implementation into the project design (rather than relying largely on
IDA supervision to maintain momentum).
(4) Restrict project activities to those which have a constituency that would ensure implementation.
11. Indications of borrower commitment and ownership
(1) GOB has already put the personnel to implement the Project in place by: establishing the Matching Grant Facility
and identifying the eminent person to serve as its Chairman (to be announced shortly upon Credit approval);
appointing Government members and welcoming the private members of the Steering Committee, the Coordination
Unit, and the MGF Board; and naming the Project Directors
(2) In advance of IDA Credit approval, GOB has expeditiously. approved Technical Assistance Project Proformas
for each of the four subprojects; prepared temis of reference for three years of external technical support; identified
and short-listed qualified consulting firms through international advertising; invited, received, and evaluated
proposals; and selected four experienced international firms: International Business and Technology Consultants Inc.
of the US for the coordination function, the ISO-9000-certified Trade Development Institute of Ireland for the MGF
management, Maxwell Stamp plc of the UK for the trade policy subproject, and Clapp & Mayne Inc of the US for
NBR/Customs support.
(3) In the Customs area GOB has taken or promised key prior steps (see Annex 2D, section 1). In particular it has:
(a) adopted, on the basis of a concept paper prepared by NBR staff and management and endorsed in principle by
IDA and other donors, a Revenue Administration Modernization Program which includes this Project's CM-1
subproject as the first phase of reform in the Customs; (b) convinced the World Customs Organization that there is
the critical mass of reform-readiness necessary to mount a WCO Customs Reform and Modernization Programme
for Bangladesh as part of the Project; (c) arranged external support in several complementary areas: (i) from IDA in
operationalizing other aspects of the RAMP, including the early implementation of a unique taxpayer identification
number scheme, groundwork for which was already laid in 1997/98, (ii) from UK DFID in strengthening and
modernizing VAT administration during 1999-2002, and (iii) from the IMF in implementing a large taxpayer unit
within the next few months; (d) facilitated implementation of the envisioned reforms by appointing/identifying: (i) an
outstanding administrator from outside the revenue services as NBR Chairman, (ii) a senior change manager from
within the services as Member for Administration & Reform, and (iii) several pro-reform middle-management staff as
change agents; (e) undertaken serious consideration with a view to introducing in FY1999/00: (i) pre-shipment
inspection under proper contractual arrangements, (ii) simplification of the Customs Tariff structure to reduce the
scope for discretionary assessments, and (iii) several process reforms which do not depend on extensive Project TA;
and (f) announced its intention to introduce legislation by FY2000/01 to provide the framework for modern, efficient
taxation and Customs services Fortuitously, the availability of an interim technical solution to the Y2k problem will
enable IDA approval for some of the equipment to occur only after an initial period of process reform that will
further demonstrate GOB/NBR commitment to the program
(4) GOB has directed and enabled the Tariff Commission to prepare for a high-level review of relative protection
among all sectors of the economy - to be held in consultation with IDA (and the IMF) within FYl999/2000 and in
subsequent years, as the basis for accelerating progress towards a trade-neutral regime.
(5) To deal with the perennial problem of training TA being wasted when officials move agencies before the benefits
of training them can be fully realized, GOB will take strenuous measures to keep trainees appropriately assigned.
This involves difficult changes in civil service rules and practice As a token of its commitment, GOB has agreed to
fund replacement training quickly if lost trainees eviscerate capacity building, and plans to use five other practical



Project Appraisal Document                                                                       Page 17
Country: Bangladesh                                                        Project Title: Export Diversification Project
means to help avoid such waste, namely: (i) selection of candidates likely to remain in post or skill area, (ii) advance
notification of training plans to the Ministry of Establishment for insertion in individuals' files, (iii) making
individuals' bonds against post-training departure skill area-specific rather than service-wide, (iv) providing for
promotion within agency or at least skill area wherever possible, and (v) requiring advice on training plans from the
project advisory teams.
12. Value added of Bank support
IDA's involvement in the Government's implementation of the matching grant facility, Customs reform agenda, and
other promotional and capacity building initiatives that require technical assistance should.
(1) facilitate inter-Ministry cooperation, and support "export culture" within/among GOB agencies;
(2) provide legitimacy for rationalized assistance to export production and marketing through clear specification of
objectives, criteria, and externalities,
(3) encourage private/public partnership and private association initiative in export promotion activities;
(4) by addressing supply-response issues, support the PFP/ESAF process in maintaining pressure for trade and
exchange policy reform to gradually reduce the need for the duty-relief and product-market development activities
supported by the Project, making them interim rather than permanent compensatory measures;
(5) provide ready access to international best practice in tax/Customs reform, export promotion, and technical
support activities; and
(6) coordinate interagency cooperation among the United Kingdom (DFID), WTO, WCO, UNCTAD, ITC and other
donors, including within the Integrated Framework-for Trade-Related Technical Assistance for Least Developed
Couintries.
Summary Project Assessments
13. Economic Assessment      [] Cost-Benefit Analysis     [] Cost Effectiveness  [x]  Other.
Analysis:               Qualitative
For a catalytic project to promote innovation and learning, to build supportive institutional arrangements, and to
reduce cost-raising constraints on export business, formal quantitative Cost-Benefit or Cost-Effectiveness Analyses
would be inappropriate. Three important qualitative assessments may be made, however:
(a) Investment in innovation and export growt7t
(I) The Matching Grant Facility represents a very small investment in the export sector It will iniect about $15
million of public funds into the private commercial market for export services over three years -- $12 million in
cash and about $3 million worth of direct technical advice. Through the prescribed criteria and appropriate
management, much of the injection should be placed at the margin where it induces private investments in
innovation that would not otherwise occur. But even if only two-thirds of the investments met that criterion, the net
addition (by facility plus firm) to investment in new product or market development would be $19 million (= 15 +12
x 2/3). This is less than 0.5% of current annual export revenue, although it is 2% of revenue excluding the
dominant garments and knitwear and the traditional jute.
(2) On the basis of experience elsewhere (see Annex 2C), such modest national investments appear to yield
extremely high returns, generating annual net export income of many times their initial value. Such growth occurs
both through utilizing unemployed resources and by shifting resources from less efficient import-substitution and
non-tradable activities. These effects are not easy to measure, since the estimate of attributable export growth itself
depends on strong assumptions about the roles of product and market developmental activities compared with the
other necessary conditions and contributing factors to export growth. Nevertheless, it is expected that the average
annual export increase would be no less than 6 times the amount of the grant (Argentina expects 7 times and the
Annex cites much higher ratios for Indonesia and Kenya),
(3) Other indicators, depending on the objective of each specific matching grant, will include. (i) competitiveness



Project Appraisal Document                                                                        Page 18
Country: Bangladesh                                                         Project Title: Export Diversification Project
improvements, both quantitative such as production costs, output per unit labor, and market share and production
costs, and qualitative, such as adoption of new management process, launch of new products, new distribution
channels, and use of consultants for problem solving, and (ii) product quality improvements, such as quality
certifications or increased price/reduced penalties on a product.
(4) Even if the direct return through the beneficiary firms' export performance were measurable, it would greatly
understate the full return to the economy by ignoring important externalities in the form of demonstration effects of
the pioneer beneficiaries' success in exploring new approaches. It would also fail to capture the critical reduction in
the economy's vulnerability over the longer term which may be due in large part to any significant diversification
that the Project will assist.
(b) Improved business environment
An important proposition of the Project's rationale is that the sort of entrepreneurial zeal which the MGF is
intended to operationalise can be easily thwarted - and thus the extra export growth limited -- by impediments in the
business environment of the sort which other Project components will be addressing If the expected impact of the
MGF on export growth is significant but hard to quantify, however, the impacts of the project components to build
trade management capacity in the public sector are even more so. It must suffice to note the reasons for expecting
high returns from these activities, rather than attempting to measure them.
(1) Existing exporters, when asked to rank obstacles to their activities, generally cite Customs procedures as a
major cost-raising and/or time-consuming constraint. It follows from this observation - and the facts that margins
are often slim and required response times short in competitive global markets -- that lower-cost and speedier import
and export procedures would enable a significant volume of additional exports which are now deterred altogether by
the present penalty. This effect may occur quite quickly as procedures are streamlined. Perhaps more significant,
however, will be its longer term impact if, as intended, the Customs Phase 1 subproject successfully pilots and
facilitates: (a) further phases of Customs reform, generalizilg the new procedures and attitudes to all importers, and
(b) equally far-reaching parallel reforms in the other revenue administrations. Each of these developments should
further lower the cost of doing business and make the tax system more equitable for the economy as a whole.
(2) The strengthening of national capacity in protection analysis is expected to facilitate (though not guarantee) the
resumption of trade liberalization and hence a significant shift in relative incentives to reduce the present bias
against export production at world prices within a protected economy. Tariff reduction should even yield benefits
for producers who are already able to enjoy world-priced imported inputs through EPZ, SBW or DDB facilities,
through the exchange rate effect of liberalization on the relative values of export proceeds and domestic inputs.
(3) Improving the national use of regional and global trade cooperation arrangements should facilitate further
expansion of trade, both through better global market access and through encouraging regional specialization which
guides domestic resources towards activities in which Bangladesh enjoys comparative advantage among its
neighbors. It should also, like the general trade liberalization, lower the cost of imported inputs to benefit exporters
and import competing producers alike. Subject in degree to other policy and market conditions, these producer
benefits from trade will in turn yield higher investment, wages, and tax revenue.
(c) Public/private partnership
Apart from the issue of the expected economic return from the actual investments made or induced by the Project in
the complementary categories of product or market development and supportive institution building, there is an
important economic consideration in the approach which it takes to the first category, PMD. The private/public
partnership instrument to encourage innovation is found in most developed and now many developing countries.
But it has seen little use so far in Bangladesh where centralized public promotion mechanisms and financial or
credit subsidies have been more common industry policy tools. Unlike the latter, the MGF approach allocates
resources and induces supply at the market level, replaces targeting by self-selection, encourages beneficiary
investment in learning and commitment to implementation, avoids distorting financial markets, has wvell-defined exit
rules and, perhaps most important, gets Government into the role of helping develop the market for business
services rather than trying to provide them itself



Project Appraisal Document                                                                    Page 19
Country: Bangladesh                                                     Project Title: Export Diversification Project
14. Fiscal and Financial Assessment
(a) Fiscal impact
The Facility should involve no public expenditures outside the Credit proceeds, and relatively minor GOB inputs
are programmed for the public capacity building components. On the other hand, the Project should yield
substantial revenues in the medium to long term in the fonns of: profit and wage taxation from the additional export
business it will enable, customs duty and VAT on the extra imports which the exports can finance; and customs
duty and VAT on imports generally, which the Customs administration reform should direct to the Treasury instead
of being diverted to unofficial recipients or otherwise lost through under-valuation and leakage. The profit tax
effect will be muted by the present generous tax holiday policy (zero for some and half-rate for other exporters), but
the phasing out of that policy may be facilitated politically by the increased profitability that should come from the
reduced anti-export bias which the Project may facilitate.
(b) Financial
For the reasons discussed in the above economic assessment, the financial returns from the investment of US$48
million cannot be quantified but are expected to be considerable. The magnitudes of IDA and other financial
support for the various subprojects have been derived from estimates of the amount and types of advisory services,
training activities and associated cost items needed to achieve the intended amount of capacity building in the
agencies concerned (in the cases of Customs and BTC/MOC for PATC) or to responsibly execute project activities
(in the cases ofthe MGF and PCU) The expencliture plan over the threeyears is given i4niAnex 4.
15. Technical Assessment
(l) The Project design places considerable responsibility for the technical quality of the advisory, training, capacity
building, grant appraisal and other services to be delivered on the four consultancy groups which GOB is
contracting for the purpose Both MOC (for PCU, PATC and MGF subprojects) and NBR (for Customs) have
made vigorous and careful efforts to invite qualified international firms to bid for the contracts under IDA
guidelines for international competitive bidding. The evaluation of the bids has been conducted responsibly, and
contracts signed with their effectiveness coinciding with that of the IDA Credit. This does not preclude the terms of
reference being amended as necessary to reflect any GOB/IDA agreements on the scope of the teams' work.
(2) Information technology systems and equipment are significant only in the Customs subproject, and to a much
lesser extent in PATC. Although procurement of the new information technology equipment for Customs needs to
be expedited, it is intended that the Consultants will, upon mobilization, review the specifications that were
appraised by the ADB when it was thought the latter's Port Project would be the main source of funding. In
addition, appropriate agreements on the plan for ASYCUDA++ or an alternative system installation have to be
reached among IDA, NBR, the Consultants when mobilized, and the system supplier.
16. Institutional Assessment
(a) Executing agencies
(1) Formally, the executing agencies are the NBR for the subproject to begin modernizing Customs and the
Ministry of Commerce for all other aspects including overall coordination, the PATC, and the MGF subproject. In
practice, the NBR is expected to exercise close supervision of its subproject, which affects all areas of Customs
operations and potentially the other tax wings too. The MOC's executive responsibilities, on the other hand, will be
largely delegated to Project entities or other agencies: the PCU will be responsible for ensuring appropriate
interaction among the agencies and subproject consultancy and implementation teams, and between them and IDA,
servicing the Technical Committee and reporting through it to the Steering Committee;
the PATC will be located in the Tariff Commission which will assume day-to-day responsibility for it, including
services to MOC and coordination of the IDA and parallel UK-DFID Projects, the MGF will be supervised by its
own Board, which has been established with five private and two official voting members; and, once they are
developed and under implementation, other PMD subprojects will be executed by the agency concerned (e.g. the



Project Appraisal Document                                                                     Page 20
Country: Bangladesh                                                      Project Title: Export Diversification Project
Department of Fisheries, an industry association, etc.) Such delegation is desirable to protect the Project activities
from excessive bureaucratic procedures and the risk of frequent changes in management personnel, while not
reducing either their ability to strengthen MOC and BTC technical capacity or the effective execution of the MGF
and the substantive PCU functions.
(2) The Government has taken a responsible decision to avoid insisting on IDA commitment to Project support for
official agencies which have nominal mandates to provide services to promote export, and to request instead the
reservation of funds to be utilized only when appropriate subprojects are identified with help from the PCU to
ensure prior or parallel institutional reform where necessary. Initially US$1 million is reserved for this purpose, but
this may be increased from "unallocated" Credit proceeds if justified by good proposals
(b) Project management
(1) Management arrangements will reflect the above executing responsibilities. While overall coordination
including accounting and reporting functions lie with the PCU, most project management will occur at the
subproject level -- i.e. Customs, PATC, the MGF, other subprojects when they are developed, and the PCU with
respect to its own substantive and housekeeping activities. Agreements of the clear division of specific management
functions between the PD for the subproject and the consulting team leader (i.e. Chief or Senior Technical Advisor
or MGF Manager) should be reached soon after the latters' mobilization.
(2) In the case of the MGF, it is expected that the Board will delegate appraisal and approval responsibility as far
as possible to the management team, confining itself to providing transparent policy guidance and protecting the
team's technical judgements from political or other influence. Since the team will not include any GOB official as
subproject director, the PD for Coordination (who is also a member of the MGF Board) will serve in that capacity
where needed, notably in the signing of grant checks on the authority of the Board or Manager.
(3) For Customs, an innovative functional arrangement for sharing responsibilities and ensuring internal ownership
and sustainability has been devised by NBR- the PD will be the NBR's "change manager" as Member for
Administration and Reform, able to keep the Chairman, fellow Members and senior Customs officials informed and
closely involved in the reform process; the team leader (international consultant on Customs modernization) will
represent the contracted firm in ensuring quality of all international and local consulting inputs and of their own
advisory, training and other outputs, as well as in coordinating the team members' and the WCO experts' activities;
and a group of Customs officials under an Implementation Manager will be seconded to the consulting team and
charged with facilitating active involvement of Commissioners and staff of Customs houses. The officials are
expected to return to NBR service at the end of the Project to help the Board ensure the sustainability and
continuation of the reform process.
(4) The BTC official designated as PD for the PATC subproject will probably serve in the same capacity for the
DFID's parallel TPCB project, and the PATC team leader (senior technical advisor) will play the key role in
coordinating the short-term TPCB inputs with those of the PATC.
(5) Although no prior arrangement has been made formally for the continuation of the PCU's analytical functions
after the Project, this should emerge in the course of its own work to help GOB develop improved official capacity
for export promotion and development. Possibilities include a public/private managed entity to assume the
promotion role of the EPB but focussed on policy and regulatory analysis, facilitation, and market information.
(c) Traininig
A major reason for TA projects failing to fully achieve their common objective of sustainably enhancing public
agencies' technical capability is the propensity of officials to move to other agencies (or leave the Civil Service)
after TA funds have been spent on training them in skills which the Government needs to retain. In the present case
this risk is not great in the large Customs subproject, since most of the audit and other skills to be learned have
value in all areas of the revenue services to which NBR officials may transfer. It is substantial, however, in the
efforts to develop public sector expertise in the complex areas of trade negotiations and protection analysis,
affecting BTC and MOC staff. This Project introduces in Bangladesh an major incentive for necessary changes to
be made in Civil Service rules, or at least practice, to reduce this risk - i e the provision for GOB to fund
replacement training when subsequent transfers of Project-trained officials significantly reduce the sustainability of



Project Appraisal Document                                                                       Page 21
Country: Bangladesh                                                        Project Title: Export Diversification Project
the TA. Short of formal changes in the rules, inappropriate transfers may be discouraged also by the provision of all
training plans to the Establishment Ministry, making it aware of individuals being trained under the Project.
17. Social Assessment
The Project's social impact will be confined to the implications of the growth in producers' business that may be
caused by those matching grants which achieve their objectives. Employment should expand, particularly of women
and particularly in industries other than RMG which now accounts for most female employment growth. Since it is
tempting for Bangladesh firms to employ readily available child workers, care will need to be taken in administering
the MGF to ensure that this practice is not condoned. As in previous projects, such care will be exercised by
requiring in advance of grant approval, and checking by field survey, that beneficiary firms are in compliance with
Bangladesh labor laws. The MGF Statement of Policies and Operating Procedutres (SPOP) has been worded
accordingly (see Annex 2A). None of the public capacity building activities will have direct social effects.
18. Environmental Assessment              Category:       [I  A         [] B          [xI C
Environment-friendly production technologies may be encouraged as a side-effect of efforts under the MGF and
new subprojects to develop export markets wliich increasingly reject products made in "dirty" factories Where that
is not the case, specific activities to be assisted will need to be evaluated in regard to whether they could promote
water or air polluting production. In particular, MGF-supported pre-investment studies should incorporate a
section on environmental management, which would include reviews of national environmental requirements --
especially those established under the Environmental Conservation Act (ECA), 1995, and the Environmental
Conservation Rules (ECR), 1997 - applicable to the proposed investment and of the relevant guidelines from the
World Bank's Pollution Prevention and Abatement Handbook (PPAH), as well as a proposed plan of the steps
necessary to obtain the required Environmental Clearance Certificate from the Department of the Environment,
indicating information needs, responsibilities, resources and timing. The SPOP has been worded accordingly, and
copies of the ECA, ECR and PPAH will be made available to applicants by the MGF management Here too, none
of the capacity building activities will have direct effects.

20. Sustainability
The Project has been designed to facilitate changes in bureaucratic practices which should be sustainable due to
their popularity with the business community and the latter's increased participation in management of export-
related support services The incentive for minimizing transfers of trained officials should significantly enhance
sustainability. The MGF is expected to be a temporary learning instrument to initiate a process of diversification
which should soon develop its own momentum (see Section I on objectives and indicators)

22. Possible Controversial Aspects
(1) The Customs subproject, although limited to piloting rather than fully implementing far-reaching changes in
the organization and culture of the administration, may nonetheless attract opposition from staff and clients who
would stand to lose from the further steps that would effectively preclude corrupt behavior NBR may require
strong political support to initiate some aspects of the reform program As indicated above, the highest risks relate
to the present and future Governments' commitment to sustain the reform process during and well beyond the
Project period, so that the ground laid here will, with further assistance, bear the expected fruit of a modem,
corruption-free revenue administration. However, both the risks and the limitations on available mitigations are
lower now than in the past - or perhaps in future if the present window of opportunity is not seized.
(2) Objective control of Matching Grant schemes elsewhere has sometimes lapsed when political or industrial
interests have been able to influence the flow of funds. Although the Bangladesh facility has been designed
carefully to minimize this risk, vigilance will be required to ensure that the appropriate balance is maintained
between semi-automatic eligibility and careful monitoring to avoid abuse.
Main Loan Conditions
23. Effectiveness Conditions.- Standard legal conditions only.
24. Other.- Important undertakings of the Government, which were agreed at Negotiations, include:
(1) MGF administration.- Throughout the Project, GOB and FBCCI will exercise due diligence to minimize the
risks of political or other interference in the objective administration of the Matching Grant Facility.
(2) Protection reviews.- GOB in consultation with IDA (and the IMF) will conduct high-level reviews of relative
protection among all sectors of the economy each year, as a basis for accelerating progress towards a neutral
regime to occur in the 2000/01 and subsequent Budgets.
(3) Customs reform.- (a) By June 2001, the Government will have introduced legislation to revise the Customs
Act and other laws that may need to be modernized to authorize the piloting or mainstreamning of new structural,
cultural and technical approaches to revenue administration. (b) Within FY2000/0 1 or earlier if process reforn
and monitoring quality permit, (i) SBW facilities will be available to most industries including partial exporters,
and (ii) the cash compensation scheme for indirect exporters will be phased out in favor of efficient drawbacks.
(4) Staff training.- Agreement to reduce risk of subsequent transfer of Project-trained officials, denying their
agency the benefit of the training, and to fund replacement training where necessary.
(5) Mid-term review.- By December 2000, MOC, NBR and FBCCI will conduct with IDA and collaborating
agencies a mid-term review of project implementation and, if necessary, take action to modify the project scope.
