Annex 2A
Statement of Policies and Operating Procedures
(SPOP) for the Matching Grant Facility (MGF)
Objective and Essential Concepts
Objective. The Matching Grant Facility (MGF) is the m-ain element of Component I, Product and Market Development
Support (PMDS) of the Bangladesh Export Diversification Project, funded by the World Bank Group's International
Development Association ODA). The MGF will support the Government of Bangladesh's objective of diversifying and
developing exports. The MGF will serve this objective by encouraging private firms to make the fullest possible use of
external business development services (local and foreign). and o'f travel, in order to enhance their ability to compete in
export markets.
Three Instruments. Experience elsewhere indicates that firm-level supply response depends on the speed at which
each firm is able to build within itself the different skills, know-how, expertise, and market understanding required by
the fast-changing economic realities external to the firm An effective method for building these capacities within the
firm is to buy external business development services. The MGF will encourage an active market in business
development services by means of a combination of three instruments
-  Thefirst instrument consists of providing to firms free hand-holding assistance on how to go about making the best
possible use of business development services, in order to inject the skills, know-how, etc. that will build
competitiveness in new products or new markets. This assistance 'AiIll ensure optimum use of the second instrument. It
is intended to build awareness and/or confidence in enterinjg what is, for many firms, the new and unknown activity of
buying business development services.
-  The second, the core of the fund, consists of the provisions of service user grants. Private enterprises will receive
grants to cover half the costs of using business developmenit services A broad range of services is covered, including
marketing, production, skills development and general lbusiness strategy.
-  The third instrument further encourages an active local Bangladesh market in business development services by the
provision of service developer grants Local associations, or other organizations, wishing to build their capacity to
supply such services to the local market will receive grants to cover half the costs of developing them. This is intended
to match the increased demand for services with a local response on the supply side of this rrarket.
SPOP. This Statement of Policies and Operating Procedures (SPOP) specifies agreements reached under the project on
exactly how the MGF is to operate. This SPOP will have legal standing as an attachment to the IDA Credit documentation.
It has been approved by IDA and the Government of Bangladesh ahead of IDA Credit Approval.
Basic Structure of the Scheme
Resources. The MGF for individual firms and groups of exporters will have available to it funding of US$10million.
There will be additional funding of US$2 million for service developer grants to providers.6 These MGF funds are to be
committed within a three-year period. Matching contributions from the recipients make the total potential value of the
scheme US$24 million.
6 The US$12 million from IDA is part of a PMDS allocation of about USS 1 6 8 million which also wvould cover $3 8 million for MGF
advisory and administration services and USSI million (subject to incicase) for other subprojects to improve support for PMD



Project Appraisal Document                                                                       Page 40
Country: Bangladesh                                                        Project Title, Export Diversification Project
Impact. The overall impact of the scheme will be evaluated in terms of the project objective to be served, namely the
capacity of private firms assisted to diversify and develop exports. The impact on each individual firm will be evaluated
primarily in terms of the degree to which firms were able to translate scheme support into increased sales. As an acceptable
working target for impact, the scheme will aim for additional and continuing average annual sales of US$6 for every $1 of
once-only grant support, directly attributable to the supported firm-level plan, and to be achieved within two years of each
grant disbursement. Once the scheme has completed operations, the impact on additional annual sales will be translated into
an assessed overall impact on export diversification and development.
Level. Each individual grant to a recipient firm must be matched 50/50 by an equal contribution by the firm itself. No
preferential proportions will be given to particular sectors, types of service, finn size, firm category, or region.
Industry Cap. No single sub-sector or industry may obtain in excess of 10% of the total MGF funding of US$12million.
The following are counted as single industries: woven garments, knitted garments, specialized textile and household linen,
jute and jute products, leather and leather products, frozen foods. The cap may be changed upon agreement between the
MGF Board and IDA.
MGF Board. The MGF Board has been set up by the Government of Bangladesh charged with supervising and operating
the scheme. The Board consists of two public-sector representatives, four private-sector nominees, plus an independent
chairman. One public-sector representative represents the Ministry of Finance and the other represents the Ministry of
Commerce. One of the four private-sector nominees is nominated by FBCCI. The other three are selected on a rotating
basis from among representatives of export-oriented industry associations, in close consultation with the associations and
ensuring balance among different types of industry. The independent chairman is appointed by agreement between the
Ministry of Commerce and FBCCI When appointed, the Chief Technical Adviser attached to the Project Coordination and
Development Unit (PCU) will sit on this board ex-officio, as will the MGF Manager (see below) who provides secretariat
services to the Board
Board Meeting Quorum. The quorum for a meeting of the MGF Board to be valid is one public-sector representative plus
two private-sector nominees Each board member may nominate an alternate to represent him/her at subsequent board
meetings. Replacement of alternates must be approved by the Chairman before they begin attending meetings.
Initial Board Responsibility. The first task ofthe Board is to adopt this agreed SPOP as its manual to direct the operations
of the scheme following IDA Credit Approval.
Management Contract. The whole scheme, including its three instruments, will be run under a term contract by a
management contractor, providing the services of a small team, consisting of an expatriate MGF Manager plus a team of
expatriate and locally-recruited staff. This team would form the MGF Management Unit, headed by the Manager. The
Ministry of Commerce was responsible for the process of selecting and appointing the contractor, by means of a process of
competitive tender acceptable to IDA.
Eligibilitv
Eligibility Criteriafor Grant Support
This section of the SPOP defines "eligible firm," "eligible export diversification/development plan," "eligible service/travel
usage," and "eligible expenditures"
T7he Eligible Firm
For service user grants, only private and NGO-sponsored commercial entities are eligible. In accordance with the
established principle of "national treatment," both locally-owned and foreign-owned firms will be equally eligible.
Parastatals, government entities and other firms where the government or its agencies have effective management control, by



Project Appraisal Document                                                                            Page 41
Country: Bangladesh                                                           Project Title: Export Diversification Project
means of being the largest shareholder or by means of having a board majority, are not eligible. Indirect ("deemed')
exporters must provide evidence that their products are inputs for exporters.
For service development grants, any legal entity not already receiving significant government funding towards its regular
operating costs is eligible. This is intended to include business and industry associations, developing export support senices
to sell to their members, as well as private and NGO-sponsored commercial entities offering such services.
For either grant type, firms engaging in employment, environmental or other practices that do not conform with Bangladesh
laws shall not be eligible, unless the activity proposed for assistance will promote such conformity.
The Eligible Export Diversiflcation/Development Plan
To be eligible for grant support, the essential criterion is that the applicant firm must have written down a realistic plan for
the diversification and/or development of its export business, taking it from its present situation to some planned future
situation. This future situation must represent a significant enhancement of its ability to compete. There must be
demonstrable additionality in what is planned Merely continuing current efforts in the hope of extending sales will not be
sufficient. The plan must be realistic in the sense that it must have a reasonable chance of being implemented successfully,
given the realities of the firm's current situation. There is no requirement that the detailed specifics ofthe plan are to be
"approved" or vetted. However, the plan must demonstrate that the firm has properly considered the following essential
planning issues:
  why one particular diversification and/or development direction was chosen instead of others; on what assumptions this
direction depends; and why this development direction can be considered realistic and achievable;
  what steps are required for that development;
  what resources are 1ikely to be needed within these steps, and where will they come from;
*  how long will the process take; and what benefits are to be expected.
The degree of detail required in a particular application will depend on the size and complexity of the application. A small
firm applying for a $ 1000 grant to send an employee on a short technical course would, for instance, only be expected to
submit a one-page plan, explaining how such an activity could reasonably be expected to benefit the planned diversification
and/or development of the firn's exports. In contrast, a large firm applying for a grant of $50,000 would be expected to
address all the above points in considerable detail
The Three Typical Stages of a Plan. Typically, a plan aimed, for instance, at expanding sales into a new market, or at
developing sales of a new product, might take a firm through these three stages:
* investigation of the target market, typically starting with desk research or limited phone research, followed then by field
interviewing, either carried out by the firm's staff or by an outside service supplier;
* supply package adaptation, on the basis of information collected from the market, in order to bring what is being
supplied presently into line with customer preferences, and to meet existing competition; for instance through changes
in product design, rationalising production to reduce costs, or through improved quality assurance, with the adaptation
completed;
*  active selling of the improved supply package.
Plans for Service Development Grants. In the particular case of grants for service development, the scheme will support
the development of new services, or the enhancement of existing services, aimed at assisting client firms in areas relevant to
export diversification and/or development. In this context, a plan should, in addition to the general requirements,
demonstrate that the services to be developed are likely to be saleable, and are likely to assist the target group of firms in
diversifying and/or developing their export business.
Pre-investment Studies. In the particular case of support for pre-investment studies, the requirement that the plan be
realistic will mean that, additionally, the applicant should demonstrate a capacity to raise the level of funding likely to be
required So, for instance, a promoter of a multi-million investment proposal, having equity capital resources available of



Project Appraisal Document                                                                       Page 42
Country: Bangladesh                                                        Project Title: Export Diversification Project
only a few thousand would not be supported with a grant. In addition, pre-investment studies should incorporate a
section on environmental management, which would include review of national environmental requirements.
Plans supported Sequentially. Once support to a particular plan has been completed, and the plan is in the process of
being satisfactorily implemented by the firm concemed, then the firm would be free to submit a further follow-on plan for
support
7he Eligible Service/Travel Usage
Any usage of an extemal service which is charged for, or of travel, and which can reasonably be expected to contribute to
the achievement of an eligible export diversification/development plan, itself will constitute an eligible service/travel usage.
The following examples of service/travel usage that would normally be eligible under this definition are provided purely for
guidance. The list is not intended to be exhaustive. In-factory consultancy and short-term contract management services,
say up to six months in duration per introduced expert, would normally be eligible, but new pernanent staff appointments
would not.7 The length oftime for which temporary contract management could be supported would depend on a case being
made as to how long the required transfer of know-how, skills, etc. could reasonably be expected to take.
Consultancy or short-term contract management assistance directed specifically at improving labor productivity or yields on
raw materials, in order to enter new markets, would normally be eligible. Assistance in improving in-house capabilities in
design-related techniques such as pattern making, prototyping, grading, sizing, counter-sampling, etc. would normally be
eligible. Buying in a set of designs for one season only, with no longer-term enhancements aimed for, would normally not.
The expenses of exposure tours for factory personnel, to view alternative techniques and technologies in factories elsewhere
would normally be eligible, as would market exploration tours by marketing personnel, to explore new export market oppor-
tunities.
Defined Deliverables from an EligibleActivity
For each eligible service/travel usage for which grant support is being sought, the firm concerned will be expected to define
at least one "deliverable," the sight of which will verify that the activity had indeed taken place, as described. Depending on
the circumstance, a defined deliverable might consist, for instance, of a detailed engineering drawing; or a prototype,
incorporating quality improvements; a training certificate; a set of standard times developed for a production process,
against which productivity could be assessed; or an externally-comrnissioned market report. Normally, a deliverable would
consist of an output produced for good reasons anyhow, rather than an output produced solely for verification purposes.
The MGF Management Unit will have the right to view/study deliverables, but not to take them offthe client firm's
premises, nor to take copies or photographs.
Eligible Expenditures
Service/Travel Usage Costs. Expenditures on service fees, and expenditures on travel and incidental expenses charged at
cost, incurred wholly and exclusively on an eligible service/travel usage will be eligible for 50% grant support. Salaries for
staff employed by the supported firmn are not eligible, nor are recurrent costs such as royalty fees. No expenditures on
hardware can be supported, even in cases where equipment or instrumentation purchases constitute an significant
expenditure within a firm-level plan s
Limits per Recipient. No single recipient firm or group of firms may receive total service user grants in excess of a
cumulative limit of US$200,000. No single recipient firm or group of firms may receive total service development grants
in excess of a cumulative limit of US$50,000. In this context, firms are considered to be a group if their annual accounts
are reported together in one document, or if they are under the effective management control of a single board or entity. In
the case of associations the service development grant would have a cumulative level of $200,000.
7 Note that training for new staff appointments would be eligible
8 Note that subsidies on hardware purchases tend to be more sensitive to countervailing action by trading partners than is cost-sharing on
business development services.



Project Appraisal Document                                                                          Page 43
Country: Bangladesh                                                          Project Title: Export Diversification Project
Exclusion. Expenditures on services provided by the firm undertaking the management contract to run the MGF, or by
associated firns, will not be eligible for support
Supplier Selection. The choice of service supplier will be made by the recipient firm, and will not be restricted to suppliers
on any central register. However, the MGF Manager will seek to satisfy himself/herself as to the supplier's competence for
the task intended, and that there is a genuine arms-length conmmercial relationship between supplier and user.
Over-charging and Collusion. The MGF Manager will also seek to satisfy himself/herself that fee rates being charged for
services are broadly in line with market realities, particularly in a case where the service supplier is not previously known to
him In exceptional cases, where he has real grounds for suspecting over-charging or collusion, grant support can be made
conditional on an acceptable process of competitive bidding between at least three technically qualified contenders.
The Recipient's Contribution
Applications for grant support are accepted on the explicit understanding that the recipient entity will match the 50% grant
contribution from the scheme with an equal contribution from its own resources. This is to ensure a strong sense of
ownership and a strong motivation to obtain value for money from the activity supported. remains with the recipient, in its
own terms.
Group Activities. Group service-using activities covering more than one firm, for instance those sponsored by trade
associations or suppliers of services, will be supported. But applications will be evaluated separately for each individual
firm. In particular, in order to ensure commitment by each supported firm, grant support will have to be matched by direct
50% contributions by each participatingfirm to the costs of providing group-based services to that particular firm. Thus,
service user grants could not be provided to match contributions from association funds or public funds. Note that this
restriction does not apply to service development grants, where contributions by association funds would be fully
acceptable.
Avoiding Double Subsidies. Similarly, usage of non-commercial service suppliers will be on the basis that double
subsidising of activities is to be avoided. In such cases, grants will be calculated so that the total subsidy element from all
sources external to the firm does not exceed 50% of the total direct costs to the firm of all eligible expenditures.
Avoiding Conflict of Interest. Any beneficiary of matching or non matching grant funding cannot be involved in
MGF supervision or otherwise associated with it. Firms associated with the management contractor may not be
beneficiaries, and Board members will declare and excuse themselves from cases involving industries in which they
have any interest.
Promotion Arran2ements
The MGF Management Unit will be expected to undertake specific initial activities aimed at promoting the scheme to the
widest possible audience of potential users. Initial promotion might take the form, for instance, of workshops or seminars to
be held in significant regional centres of business activity. In addition, successful experiences resulting from the scheme and
other lessons learned will be widely disseminated for the benefit of other potential exporters or grant recipients (taking care
to avoid release of proprietary or other confidential information).
Processing the Individual Grant
Letter ofAgreement
On receiving approval for grant support, each recipient will be required to sign a letter of agreement, specifying the details
of the actual support agreed. This letter will also bind the recipient to present defined deliverables for viewing by the
management unit. The letter of agreement will also specify the individual nominated by the recipient firm to be its
representative in dealings with the MGF.



Project Appraisal Document                                                                       Page 44
Country: Bangladesh                                                        Project Title: Export Diversification Project
Outputs and Confidentiality. The letter of agreem-enit will confirn that all outputs from supported activities wiUl remain the
exclusive property of the supported firm, with commercial confidentiality fully preserved. Contractor staff will be bound
under their contract to respect the commercial confidentiality of all deliverables, outputs, and infonnation supplied during
contact with the firm.
Supporting Documentation. The letter of agreement will bind the recipient to back up all claims for the reimbursement of
half of eligible expenditures with receipted invoices/vouchers for all fees and incidental expenses covered, so as to enable the
management unit to verify that the expenditures had indeed been made. This documentation, plus the agreed deliverables,
must be retained for possible inspection by auditors or WVorld Bank supervision missions for three years after each receipt of
a grant [or longer, if required by local law].
Impact Data. The letter of agreement will bind the recipient to provide continuing sales revenue data, sufficient to track the
direct impact of the grant support on the enhanced competitiveness of the firm, and thus to monitor the achievement by the
scheme of its impact target. The letter will also commit the recipient to co-operate fully with the annual audits and the
sample surveys to be conducted later to evaluate irnpact. Each recipient will also be obliged to complete, after each
supported activity, a brief one-page "feedback form," indicating the level of client satisfaction achieved by the service
supplier.
Formal Requirements. The letter of agreement will refer to the formal requirements stated in the IDA/GOB Development
Credit Agreement for the Project, which are notified to applicants in the application form, viz.
. . Grants shall be made on terms whereby the Borrower, acting tlhrough the MGF Board, shall obtain, by Letter of
Agreement with the Beneficiary, rights adequate to protect the interests of the Association, and the Borrower,
including to the extent that it shall be appropriate the right to: (a) require the Beneficiary to carry out the
proposal or program to be financed out of proceeds of the Service User / Service Development Matching Grant
with due diligence and efficiency and in accordance with sound technical, financial, managerial employment and
environmental standards and to maintain adequate records; (b) require that: (i) any services to be financed out of
the proceeds of the Credit shall be obtained at a reasonable price, account being taken also of other relevant
factors such as their quality and the competence of the parties rendering them; and (ii) such services to be used
exclusively in the carrying ouit of the proposal or program; (c) obtain all stuch information as the Association or
the Borrower shall reasonably request relating to the foregoing and to the administration, operations and
financial condition of the Beneficiary and to the benefits to be derived from the proposal or program; and (d)
suspend or terminate the right of the Beneficiary to the use of the proceeds of the Credit upon failure by such
Beneficiary to perform its obligations under its agreement with the Borrower.
Approval ofApplications
Delegated Approval. Applications will be prepared jointly bv would-be recipient firms and the MGF Management Unit
staff The MGF Board has the discretion to subdelegate its grant approval powers to the MGF Manager, either in entirety,
or up to an approval limit that the Board itself specifies
Board Approval. For categories of grant approval not subdelegated to the MGF Manager, the Manager will recommend
applications for the Board's approval. However, if the Board refuses to approve an application so recommended, or fails to
approve such an application within four calendar weeks from the recommendation, the application will be automatically
referred by the Manager to the Project Steering Committee (adv'ised by the PCU) and reported immediately to IDA. The
Steering Commnittee would have the authority to refer the application back to the MGF Board, with its comments, for
reconsideration and review by the Board. If, after that, the Steering Committee and Board could not still agree about a
recommended application, the matter would need to be resolved by discussion between IDA, the Steering Committee, the
MGF Board and the MGF Manager In the event thnat the dispute could not be resolved, the application would be deemed
not approved.
Approval by Circulation. If no board meeting can be arranged sufficiently quickly, the MGF Manager will have the
altemative available of arranging a board approval "by circulation. " Copies of the application or applications pending will



Project Appraisal Document                                                                     Page 45
Country: Bangladesh                                                      Project Title: Export Diversification Project
be circulated to board members, who would have the opportunity to object to an application within seven days. If no
objections by board members are received by the MGF Manager within seven days, the application would be deemed to
have been approved.
Potential Board Member Conflict of Interest. If an application is being considered for a firm which competes with a finn
in which a particular board member has an iilterest, then that board member is to exclude himselflherself from the
discussions covering that particular application.
Information provided to the MGF Board. Commercially confidential information provided by an applicant to the MGF
Management Unit would be removed from an application ahead of it being referred for board consideration. For instance,
names of foreign sales contacts would be removed, as would be specific characteristics of products under development but
not yet made public.
Rejections. Since virtually no technical evaluation is required in the approval process, and since all eligibility criteria are
designed to involve straightforward yes-or-no decisions, outright rejections are expected to be rare. However, any rejections
are to be delivered in person to the firm concemed by the MGF Manager, to give an opportunity for discussion of possible
next steps. The intention is that every applicant will be given the maximumn opportunity and assistance required so as to
enable it to benefit from the scheme.
Revoking an Approval. The board may only revoke an approval already granted by the manager in the event that false or
misleading information is determined to have been provided within an application. Any such case is to be reported in detail
to IDA within one week of the board decision.
Quarterly Review Meeting. Each calendar quarter, after receipt from the MGF Manager of the progress report, the Board
will review progress. It will also review approvals and rejections, and could then, if it wished, give guidance to the MGF
Manager on future approvals and application policies Any such guidance should be reported immediately to IDA.
Grant Payment
Basis of Payment. Payment of grants will be on a reimbursement basis, conditional on sight of defined deliverables, and of
receipted invoices/vouchers for eligible expenditures, certified by the appointed representative of the firn. Expenses for
travel outside the finn's country of residence will be calculated on the basis of published Economy/Coach class airfares, plus
per-diem allowances for accommodation and subsistence which are at a rate no more than 10% over the published UNDP
per-diem rates for the destinations concemed.
Authorization. Payments of grants out of the Special Account set up for the Export Diversification Project will require,
first, the written authorization of the MGF Manager on behalf of the Board, and second, the signing of cheques by the PD
for Coordination or his alternate and the Deputy PDC or his altemate.
Administration
Monitorng Scheme & Contractor Performance
Contractor Performance. As part of the contract, the management contractor will be committed to develop, and obtain
board approval of, an annual work plan for each twelve-month period of operation of the contract. This will specify in
detail the activities to be carried out in order to launch and promote the scheme, and to manage it under the terms of this
SPOP. The work plan will specify the man-day inputs for each activity. Progress made in achieving this plan, plus
modifications proposed to the plan, would be reported to the board for each operational calendar quarter, to be submitted
within 10 days of the end of the quarter The report should include data on. each application received, value plus one
paragraph describing the activity; each applications approved, value plus one paragraph on expected impact; each
applications rejected, one paragraph describing reasons, breakdown of approvals by subsector, size of firm, and type
of grant; number of site visits; number and value of disbursements and time elapsed after authorization; and project
implementation issues. The value of grants approved resulting from the contractor's promotional and appraisal work



Project Appraisal Document                                                                     Page 46
Country: Bangladesh                                                      Project Title: Export Diversification Project
is expected to reach US$4 million each year The contractor is also expected to gather and compile information which
would assist impact evaluation.
Impact Evaluation. A comprehensive evaluation of the scheme's impact based in part on a sample survey of recipient
firms, will be conducted between the end of the third year and the Project closing date (June 2002), enabling the results to be
incorporated into the Implementation Completion Report for the Project. The survey and evaluation will be contracted out
by the Board to an independent specialist, whose selection is to be approved in advance by IDA. The survey will include
comprehensive site visits to a minimum of 10% of grant recipients and a control group of similar firms not assisted by
the facility, and will be supplemented by data on all recipients retained by the management contractor. The evaluation
will seek not only to evaluate direct incremental impact, but also to gather information on indirect impact. The detailed
performance indicators will be agreed beforehand with IDA but could include:
*  Increased Exports: (i) the achievement of an average annual export perfornance equivalent to a multiple of the
cost of the grants paid to the firms, and (ii) a larger increase in exports among firms in the program as compared
to the control group.
*  Competitiveness Improvements: the survey will compare competitiveness indicators of assisted firms with those
of the control group, including production costs, workforce skill levels, other productivity indicators, and market
share. Other indicators will include adoption of new management processes such as export programming, new
distribution channels, launch of new products, and use of consultants for problem solving.
*  Quality Improvements: the survey will compare indicators of quality improvement among assisted firms and the
control group such as quality certifications, value increases, and rejection rate decreases.
*  Behavioral Features: customer orientation, institutionalization of quality assurance and continuing product develop-
ment, employee motivation, company image. It will also specifically seek to evaluate the willingness of firms to
continue using specialist services but at full market prices.
Interim Impact Survey. Although the final impact survey will allow all stakeholders to evaluate properly the eventual
impact of the scheme, these stakeholders will also require an interim evaluation somewhat earlier. A project-wide mid-term
review is planned for about October 2000. To assist that mid-term review, a smaller representative interim sample survey
will be conducted, based on site visits accounting for about 10% of grant funds disbursed by that time. The indicators
proposed above will be used to the extent feasible at this stage, although the analysis will necessarily focus more on the
effectiveness of the management arrangements, to confirm that grant recipients have presented adequate business
plans, and that grant funds are being used for the purposes intended, and to receive feedback from the firms
concerning the operation of the scheme. This survey and analysis also will be conducted by an independent specialist
contracted by the Board with IDA approval, who will collaborate with the IDA team responsible for the review.
Accounts andAudit Procedures
Auditing. The scheme will require an annual financial audit by independent auditors acceptable to IDA, with certified
copies of audited accounts to be subnitted to the Bank within six months of each fiscal year. The MGF Board will
therefore propose, ahead of scheme start-up, these audit details, and submit them to IDA for approval
Management Accounts and Records. Administration of the scheme by the MGF Manager will require detailed intemal
record keeping for management purposes. Formats will be set up in agreement with the Financial Management Specialist in
the Project Coordination Unit, and record keeping arrangements will need to be approved as acceptable to the appointed
auditors.
Amendments to this Manual
This manual may be modified as required, so as to achieve the objectives of the scheme, but only by recommendation of the
MGF Board and the Manager The Project Steering Committee would review such recommendations and resolve any
differences between the Board and the Manager before seeking fornal endorsement by IDA. The manual should seek to
preserve a balance between achieving the objectives of the scheme and minimizing leakage on the one hand, and preserving
simplicity, automaticity and transparency in the approvals and supervision process on the other

Matching Grant Facility
Characteristics and evolution of the exp-ort sector
The industrial sector's ability to maximize export potential has been hampered by the dominance of public services with
inherent inefficiencies, lack of awareness by exporters of international market needs as to the type and quality of products,
dependency on large intermediaries, and the absence of skills Attempts by the government to promote exports have largely
been through centralized agencies, which are seen by exporters to be bureaucratic in nature and not responsive to individual
firms' specific needs in terms of identifying markets and market demand, or in locating and reaching buyers abroad. In spite
of these constraints there has been substantial growth in some sectors resulting from opportunities created by quotas and trade
preferences (RMG, knitwear, textiles), or by exploiting natural resources (frozen seafood).
Table 3(a) illustrates the evolution of export values from 1985 to 1998 with the main export subsectors being ready made
garments, knitwear, frozen shrimp, and leather These subsectors accounted for 80% of total exports in 1997/98 with a
significant contribution to foreign exchange generation. However, the GOB considers that active promotion is required for
the development of other emerging subsectors such as fresh vegetables, cut flowers, furniture as well as for increasing the
value added of the traditional exporters (see description under sample subsectors described below). Project background
materials and working papers contain more complete descriptions of the subsectors reviewed.
Cross-cutting needs identified by exporters and estimate of demand
A variety of complementary techniques were used to identify obstacles to expont diversification in order to estimate the likely
uptake of the MGF by individual firms These included an Exporters Need Survey, numerous meetings with subsector
associations, chambers, individual exporting firms, service providers, as well as a review of over 50 letters expressing interest
in the MGF. Subsectors reviewed included ready           fr     ceyieti                            has baen

issues as child labor, hygiene and quarantine requirements in specific sectors, GSP and Certificates of Origin etc).

Efforts to promote exports in Bangladesh has been concentrated in providing support services through public organizations
such as the Export Promotion Bureau, Jute Mills Corporation, Handloom Board, Small Industries Corporation etc. Firms and
industry sectors express a lack of faith in these centralized public service

Sample of Subsectors with Issues and Needs Assessment
Shrimp
Shrimp exports from have been growing rapidly in recent years and now represent over 5% of Bangladesh imports. Since
1995 Bangladesh is the seventh largest producer with 4.21% of the total world production. Bangladesh is primarily using
block freezing for shrimp although a few processing plants are developing the capacity to produce the higher quality
Individual Quick Frozen (IQF) shrimp, or other prepared shrimp products. However, their packaging quality, primarily wax
coated cardboard box, does not reflect the "luxury" nature of the product. The robust nature of demand for shrimp and
scarcity of shrimp in the international market enabled Bangladesh to continue to export to Canada, United States and Japan
despite the temporary export ban to the EU maintaining export values while production volume decreased. Key needs are:
* Needfor Continued Quality Control. The industry continues to be vulnerable to export bans and concerns over
microbiological hazards and has started to address this issue through implementation of a system called Hazard Analysis
Critical Control Point (HACCP) to control microbiological hazards. Firms recognize the crucial nature of monitoring
hazards and many have either just installed or are in the process of installing their own testing laboratory9. As of the end
of last year, out of a total of 122 firms, 45 to 50 have already been upgraded with government assistance in access to
loans. Firms are in the process of preparing their own operational manuals and preparing their plants to implement the
HACCP system.

Readymade Garment (RMG) and Knihvear
These two subsectors provides 65% of total export earning and have been enjoying rapid growth over the past decade. Four
geographical markets represented by USA Germany, UK and France account for over 70% of RMG and knitwear exports.
The sector is still at a relatively early stage of development with buying agents supplying imported material and most of the
work done by contractors for a fee on a cut, make and trim ("CMT") basis. Industry projections are for continued growth of
20% per year over the next decade. This will be achieved in part by diversification into new markets, such as Japan, which
has grown by some 63% to $9.36 million from 1995/96 to 1996/97. Whilst these projections are encouraging for Bangladesh,
there is also some risk of a reversal if the industry cannot comply with quota/certification requirements relating to
Generalised Scheme of Preference (GSP) and Certificates of Origin. In addition, phasing out of the Multi-Fibre Agreement
(MFA) requires Bangladesh to prepare for the transition to a new competitive environment.

*  Human Resource Issues. Skills training, particularly targeted at production supervision, sewing technology and sewing
machine maintenance and repairs has been identified by most firms visited. Firms have been reluctant to train in isolation
since they are subject to losing trained workers to other firms. Firms participating collectively in training, and access to
some funding assistance for training through the MGF will help to offset some of this reluctance
* Marketing, Market Development. Firms have identified direct exploration of markets as an important activity as a way
develop a direct relationship with end buyers in order to be able to start shifting away from their current dependency on
buying houses.
Demandfor MGF: The BGMEA has been involved in determining the likely MGF uptake by its 2,000 members of the MGF.
To support this effort they have retained a group of six persons to work with firms to explain the MGF program. Based on
members' expressions of interst, BGMEA estimates that approximately 10% of its members or 200 firms will request
assistance through the MGF. To date 17 letters of interest have been received from individual firms, each with an average
estimated grant requirement ranging between $40,000 -$ 60,000 This initial interest in the MGF by the RMG and Knitwear
subsectors suggests that demand is likely to be robust resulting in a potential total demand exceeding available allocated
funding to subsectors under the MGF.
Cut Flowers
This is a small emerging export sector in its infancy stage. The common flower for export is the tuberose with the main
international competition coming from Iran and Kenya. Opportunities exist for increasing exports of cut flowers, particularly
to Middle East Markets with direct air connections.

Furniture
There are 3,952 producers of furniture in Bangladesh ranging from cottage industry to full scale production employing
approximately 400,000 persons Demand in the export market is for higlh quality products and the main international
competition is from India The Association of Furniture Producers feels that they have a competitive edge over India due to
the higher quality of Bangladesh production. In order to improve export performance in the international market more-
research must be done on new technology and production teclniques as well as improving technical expertise and providing
training in all phases of current furniture technology.. The Association estimates that the current capacity for exports is
approximately $350,000 per month through the 7 to 8 firms that they have identified as being export ready. It expects to
improve on this

Annex 2C
Experience with Matching Grants and Other
Technical and Marketing Support Systems
1. Matching Grants
The Matching Grant Facility for Bangladesh forms part of a series of such initiatives both under Bank projects and sponsored
by other donors or governments. Matching grants are used in industrialized market economies to replace previous public
support services for trade and industry, e.g the UK's Department of Trade and Industry, Austrade in Australia, and the State
of Maryland. Matching Grant schemes have been or are being successfully implemented through Bank projects in:

Completed Projects. Only the projects for India (2), Indonesia and Kenya have been evaluated since the remaining projects
are under implementation.
i India's Industrial and EXpOrt Project contained two consultancy funds - the Export Marketing Fund and the
Productivity Fund totaling US$20 million. The project was completed in 1992 having financed 359 grants totaling
US$ 16 million. The project was considered successful: the recipients of the export marketing fund and the productivity
fund reported on average an annual export growth of 68 percent and 48 percent respectively, far exceeding the national
average.
UgIndia's Export Development Project (1989) included four consultancy contracts totaling US$20 million. The project
was completed in 1995 benefiting 450 firms. The project was also considered successful: between 1992-95 the ratio of
exports to sales of the assisted companies increased from 8 8% to 11 4%. For the same period the growth of exports for
these comnpanies was 26% per annum compared to a 15% annual growth for all Indian exports.
C pThe Export Development Project for Indonesia provided US$5.1 million for an export support fund. At project
completion in 1991, the fund had assisted 397 export companies and 20 export support organizations. One major
problem was the complicated approval process resulting in a disbursement of only 68 percent of the comuitted funds by
project completion. However from the point of view of its impact the project was successful: A sample of recipient
companies reported that the value of their exports had increased 17 times the total cost of the assistance.
The Knya Export Development Project (1992 provided US$1.4 million in matching grants as a pilot project, which
were fully disbursed. Although disbursements, which were administered by the government were slow at the beginning
these became satisfactory as implementation proceeded. The achievements were highly satisfactory with 175 grant-aided
projects approved compared to a target of 105. The ratio of additional export revenues to grants was 15:1 substantially
over the target ratio specified during design of 2:1
Ongoing Projects. An ongoing evaluation of projects under implementation in Africa shows the following preliminary
results:
prSouth of the Sahara (Senegal, Uganda, Madagascar) Main problems with these projects are: (i) the matching grants
are open not only to exporters and non-exporters but also to a wide variety of groups such as farmers and women, which



Project Appraisal Document                                                                           Page 53
Country: Bangladesh                                                           Project Title: Export Diversification Project
without eligibility criteria results in a large number of non-performing projects; and (ii) supervisory groups of the
matching grant administration consist mainly of the users who use their influence to determine who are the beneficiaries.
Mauritius. Preliminary results are good. The project focussed mainly on transfer of technology to exporters. One of the
inain success areas has been in moving exporters from basic cotton knitwear to synthetics.
Lessons from the ongoing evaluation are:
(i) the need to have focussed project objectives. A main objective suggested is the transfer of technology due to its
characteristic as a public good (demonstration effect, movement of workers from leading technology companies to
followers, cost reductions which benefit consuming industries, absence of learning meclhanisms in particularly in Africa
which receives the visit of few buyers /suppliers),
(ii) the beneficiary firm must have the planning, technical and financial capability to use the grant,
(iii) firms that do not meet these conditions could receive help under a different grant scheme focusing on how to prepare
business plans and make financial projections,
(iv) the matching grant manager should be proactive in providing support and advice to those subsectors which are crucial for
the development/strengthening of other subsectors (e.g. textile industry as a provider of inputs to RMG industry), and
(v) the projects should make allowance for evaluations on the impact of the matching grants independent from the grant
managers.
2. Alternative Systems
A World Bank study of Technical and Marketing Support Systems for Successful Small and Medium-Size Enterprises in Four
Countries (Indonesia, Japan, Korea and Colombia) reached the following conclusions:
"First, the leading source of support comes from private channels - - from buyers and traders, from similar firms,
suppliers and subcontracting principals, andfrom the determined efforts of SMEs themselves. It follows that the first
order of business for SME support policy is to ensure that the private marketplace can work, that the liberal rules
govern the internationalflow of technical and marketing resources.
"Second, the benefits of private support mechanisms are not available equally to all, but vary with an SME's
,endowments': whether SMEs are embedded in pre-existing private inter-firm or community-based networks; whether
firms are pioneers in a new activity or one of many participants in an already mature subsector,; whether the country
in which they are active has a high profile in the export marketplace. Collective marketing and technological
supports turn out to be used and valued disproportionately by less well-endowed - - though subsequently successful -
- firms
"Third, the record of delivery of collective technical and miarketing support is a chequered one, but some promising new
approaches appear to be coming to the fore. The most promising interventions are those with a "light touch":
their delivery mechanisms generally are decentralized; and their goals are to support, rather than supplant, the
private marketplace - - to co-finance SME efforts to tap the marketplace for neiv marketing and technical
resources, to make available additional sources of technical information, and to facilitate communication between
firms, including between SMEs and international buyers.
"In all, the empirical results suggests that, consistent with the emphasis of the previous decade, the overall business and
incentive environment is the most important determinant of the effectiveness of marketing and technological support
systems for SMEs. However, the results also caution against complacenc, against the presuimption that a liberalized
private marketplace will be sufficient to secure industrial development
-- Brian Levy et al , World Bank Policy Research Working Paper # 1400, December 1994

Comments on the Institutional Context pre-Proect
(1) Extracts from preparation mission Customs administration expert's "general observations on the current situation
in Bangladesh" 13:
Port Congestion  Current customs cargo clearance and examination procedures in Bangladeshi limit the scope for
trade facilitation initiatives and sustained export growth. Customs business practices, attitudes, and corporate objectives
remain firmly fixed on the use of historical transaction-based policies and procedures which revolve around detailed
completion and extensive re-verification of customs related documentation prior to the release of cargoes.
11     A review of port congestion and port operations at Chittagong port noted that approximately 65% of cargo on hand
was export related. The port was very congested and vessels were waiting in the stream for available berths. There was little
container stacking in the terminal due to the need to provide ease of access for physical examination of consignment contents
by customs. As a consequence the container terminal was overflowing and additional containers were being examined and
stored in other areas of the port usually allocated for break bulk cargoes The current customs physical cargo examination
policy requires that 5% - 10% of each and every consignment be examined for classification, valuation, quantity / anti
smuggling purposes. In effect this single policy results in every consignment, whether imports or exports, being stopped for
somne form of physical examination. It has become the prime reason for current port congestion problems, adds significant
delays in getting goods to market, and accounts for at least one additional day's storage for all export related consignments
11     Such an intensive random  examination policy is not only a significant impediment to international trade
development in Bangladesh but has also created an environment of complacency in the implementation of customs
examination procedures. It is counter productive as an anti-smuggling device, and encourages the spread of corrupt practices
as traders and clearing agents negotiate faster access to individual assignments. This random examination policy has firmly
established existing customs procedures as an effective non-tariff trade barrier in Bangladesh and should be discontinued
forthwith in relation to all export consignments and import consignments destined for Special Bonded Warehouses. A more
targeted, risk management approach also needs to be adopted for other imported cargoes once staff are better equipped to
undertake more effective cargo examinations for concealed items
13 Anthony Mort, Special Bonded Warehouses and Customs Process Reform, Working Paper # 1, Novel.bcr )5, 1997.



Project Appraisal Documont                                                                             Page 57
Country: Bangladesh                                                            Project Title: Export Diversification Project
".....I It was noticed that there was a significant lack of modern investigative and examination tools available to staff
involved in physical cargo examinations. As a consequence many examinations are cursory or documentary in nature, and
achieve no useful purpose at this stage. If customs are to adopt a more effective approach to the physical examination of
consignments, as suggested elsewhere in this report, it is essential that they be better equipped to do the job. This means
effective retraining of examination teams, operation of a better equipped customs laboratory capable of producing speedy and
accurate classification of referred products, and access to the necessary tools of trade.
"...... Greater emphasis needs to be placed on developing an effective and efficient customs risk management regime to
target specific consignments, facilitate legitimate trade, and strengthen fonral revenue collection. Such risk management
regimes rely on the development and effective operation of a comprehensive national customs intelligence analysis capability
that can detect and investigate organized fraud and serious smuggling threats. While it is understood that a custom
intelligence operation does exist in Bangladesh lack of time during this mission prevented a closer examination of the status
of this side of the Bangladesh customs operation.
11     While the majority of the port congestion problems at Chittagong port are as a result of inappropriate customs
examination procedures, it must not be assumed that customs are the only agency in need of improvement. Considerable time
was spent with staff of the Chittagong Port Authority (CPA), and it was clearly evident that current attitudes, policies, and
procedures within this organization also played a major role in maintaining existing port inefficiencies. For example it is
understood that there is currently a cargo handling restriction applied per consignment of 3 tons despite the fact that the
lifting capacity within the port is far in excess of this. Consequently many split consignments exist and the benefits of
containerization are under utilized Furthermore it is also understood that CPA do not undertake Bay Plan examination prior
to the arrival of the vessel. As a consequence unloading planning takes place on the dock with resultant inevitable delays
Existing administrative and operational procedures within CPA are antiquated and in need of drastic simplification,
inodernization and automation. In many respects there appears to be a lack of commercial aggressiveness to manage and
operate the port as a key strategic asset of thle country, and to introduce productivity improvement initiatives aimed at
improving service delivery and attracting customers. This issue should be addressed as a matter of priority by Government.
"1     Simplification and harmonisation of documentation and procedures  An examination of existing customs goods
declaration processing procedures in Dhaka and Chittagong clearly showed that a successful implementation of the
ASYCUDA system had been achieved within the confines of the current operation. However a number of issues were
identified which currently contribute to slowing down the document processing line, adds confusion or unnecessary cost to the
operation, and negatively impacts on the security and integrity of the current customs declaration processing system. The
biggest revenue risk at the moment is the practice of Clearing & Forwarding Agents intermingling with customs staff while
trying to get appraisements and then acting as couriers for their own goods declarations (and associated supporting
documentation) as they move the declarations through the various stages of the examination process. This practice is open to
corruption, collusion, and theft. As a consequence it should be replaced as soon as possible with an environment whereby
Clearing & Forwarding Agents surrender their documents at the point of lodgment into the customs clearance system, and
they do not get access to them again until the end of the process when clearance is given to remove the goods from customs
control.
"1     It was noticed that a variety of different documentation formats exist for different goods declarations, and in some
cases even for the same declaration. Multiple formats of varying shapes and sizes add to production and storage costs and also
indirectly slow down the processing line as staff adjust to information being in different locations on different forms. This
area should be rationalized through the use of the common UN/ECE Layout Key format international trade documentation
(i.e. the same format as is used for ASYCUIDA input). This same documentation format should be applied for ALL import,
export, warehousing, and transit operations. By all means retain the existing color coded concept for such documentation, but
simplify and standardize the process by using the same forn design. There is an urgent need to significantly reduce the
number of signatures required to process goods declarations, with a target of no more than two signatures per document. This
should be achieved as part of the process of introducing a single common declaration format.
11     During discussions with shippers it was noticed that Bangladesh has its own unique design of forms that are used for
reporting the arrival of ships. These forms are not aligned with the International Maritime Organization's published list of
standardized facilitation forms (IMO FAL Forms I - 6) As a consequence shippers must re-format this international
information for specific use in Bangladeslh at significant cost and time wastage. The IMO FAL Forms I - 6 have been in
existence for quite a number of years now and their use by Customs Administrations hias been fully endorsed by the World
Customs Organization. Consequently at this point there appears to be no logical reason why such internationally accepted
formats should not also be used in Bangladesh Consequently it is suggested that NBR simplify and standardize the design of
all shipping documentation by authorizing the use of the IMO FAL I - 6 forms for the general declaration, cargo manifest,



Project Appraisal Document                                                                              Page 58
Country: Bangladesh                                                             Project Title: Export Diversification Project
crew list, crews effects list, ships stores etc. Similar internationally accepted format also exist for air transportation and NBR
should be encouraged to prescribe the use of such formats as accepted by the IMO, IATA & ICAO.
11     Transparency and integrity  Initial observations highlighted a general lack of transparency, integrity and confidence
in the equitable administration of customs legislation, policies, and procedures in Bangladesh. There is no short term quick
solution to rectify this perception, however it is felt tlat adoption of a system based approach to targeted risk management as
outlined in this paper will go some way towards rebuilding confidence in the tax administration capability and capacity in the
country. To assist in this process there is also a need to introduce transparency into the interpretation of Bangladesh Customs
legislation, both domestically and internationally, through publishing all related legal and operational material in both
Bengal and English languages.
"1     It is also thought prudent to develop, publish, maintain, and make available a single publication containing all non-
sensitive operational procedures, instructions, and guidelines associated with the clearance of international consignments.
This should include both the customs role and expectation and obligations falling on the traders. This will clearly spell out to
staff and the trade exactly what each others obligations are, and will also be able to act as good reference material for staff
and the public who are undergoing training in customs related procedures.
"1     It was noticed that multiple reference documents are used by customs officers in determining an effective rate of
tariff to apply to any given shipment. This results in a significant waste of time, and makes it difficult for others to easily
determine effective tariff information. It is recommended therefore that a single Customs Tariff be produced, maintained, and
published containing all applicable tariff items, tariff rates, concessionary rates, and guidelines for use
"1     Customs integrity  The current focus on detailed individual transaction based processes and procedures in all areas
of tax administration in Bangladesh has created an environment that is supportive of widespread institutionalized corruption
throughi out most levels of affected organizations including regulatory agencies and the banking sector. Corruption and
unauthorized interference in the smooth administration of regular business practices appears to have grown to such an extent
that its continued existence appears to have been accepted by many as a necessary fact of life. As a consequence cargo
clearance and tax administration processes retain significant needs for extensive human intervention with resultant greater
opportunities for corruption throughout the entire international trade cycle. Advice was received that the level of corruption in
the current system equates to approximately 5% of Letter of Credit values
The only way to address corruption on such a large scale is to simplify the processes as much as possible, reduce
areas where human intervention is required, move away from a transaction based focus in policy and procedural development,
develop appropriate self compliance incentives, and strengthen enabling legislation to balance the facilitation initiatives that
are introduced. A major priority for NBR should now be to re-establish confidence of the government and the trade
community in its tax administration capabilities This can only be achieved througlh enforcing high standards of integrity,
accountability, and responsibility throughout all NBR departments while protecting operational areas from interference in the
equitable administration of legal obligations.
Special Bonded Warehouse scheme  During the course of this mission the team visited a number of organization
which operated special bonded warehouse, and witnessed manufacturing operations ranging from sophisticated high quality
establishments to those that were less than desirable The team spent many hours with industry associations and government
agencies as we sought to understand the operational constraints on the current manufacturing sector. As a result of this
analysis it was concluded that the current historical, transaction based co-efficient focus towards bonded manufacturing
warehouse operations is impeding export diversification and development. It is therefore obvious that an alternative approach
needed to be adopted. Consequently it was suggested that a system based, risk management approach to auditing taxpayer
records at periodic intervals would be more appropriate for Bangladesh. This suggested approach was discussed at length
with industry, trade associations, and NBR personnel and their respective input was taken into account in the design of a
more stream lined approach to the operation of special bonded warehouse activities, that aims to reduce tax leakage, make
taxpayers mnore accountable, reduce the cost to trade, support export diversification and growth, while strengthening the duty
suspense system and introducing confidence into the tax administration system.............
11     Duty Drawback scheme  The historical focussing on transaction based procedural issues and multi-verification of
information is also prevalent throughout the entire duty suspense, duty drawback, and special bonded warehouse schemes
operated by Bangladesh Customs As a consequence significant delays and costs are being experienced in the processing of
duty drawbacks with a resultant negative impact on taxpayers' cash flow.
"1     Bangladesh allows drawback of customs duties on imported inputs that are used in the manufacture of goods for
export. This is achieved through a complex mechanism to establish exact duty input and output coefficients on manufactured
quantity produced. As a result of the delays experienced in calculating new drawback coefficients, coupled with the in built



Project Appraisal Documnent                                                                            Page 59
Country: Bangladesh                                                            Project Title: Export Diversification Project
delays in the drawback process itself, a fiscally neutral environment does not exist for exporters. This is because while the
exporter has to pay duties and taxes on inputs at the time of import the drawback refunds are made only after the export
proceeds have been realized and evidence of export tallies have been produced using the cargo out turn report on the Shipping
Bill. As such the exporter incurs financial costs on monies borrowed to sustain a workable cash flow.
11     As a result of this complex and disjointed approach to drawback policy it is easily open to abuse, is not transparent or
equitable to the taxpayer and remains inefficient. A key element for reform in this area is the need to change the point of
payment for drawback of duties away from the time that export earnings are repatriated, to the time that the goods are
"deemed" to have been exported by the acceptance of a Bill of Export by customs. Consequently the current constraints
imnposed on the drawback process by the exchange control regulations should be lifted. The requirement for the cargo out
turn to be sighted on the Shipping Bill as part of the refund process should be dispensed with as soon as possible as it removes
yet a further perceived justification for the current physical examination policy relating to export related cargoes.
(2)    Extract fromn World Customs Organization report on participation in the July 1998 appraisal miSSion:14
11     It was quite evident from speaking to the officers that the Bangladeshi Customs Administration was in need of
comiiprehensive reforms. The procedures and systems are complicated - a good example being the manual Customs entry form
which requires anything up to 40 signatures before goods can be released The competency and integrity of many officers,
particularly Inspectors (field officers), was very low One of the reasons given for this state of affairs was that recruitment at
Inspector levels had not been undertaken for a very long time and vacancies at those levels were being filled by promoting
staff ivho were formally helpers and general hands. This appears to have resulted in the lowering of efficiency levels as well
as aggravating integrity problems.
On the other hand, the senior officers interviewed were found to be acutely aware of the problems being faced by
their administration and how the problems impact the economy and image of the countiy. All the officers had had the
opportunity of visiting other Customs administrations and were concerned about the state of their own Customs systems. Most
lainented that, so far, there has not been any significant breakthrough in finding solutions to their'problems.
11     The conditions of service of Customs officers are not commensurate with their responsibilities. Officers interviewed
feel that as long as officers are not paid a living wage it would be difficult to curb corruption. It was interesting to note that
the majority of officers interviewed spoke openly and passionately against corniption. All of them had no doubt that, given
the right conditions of service and remuneration, most officers had enough self respect to refrain from corrupt activities. All
the officers who talked freely about the integrity issues warned that the issue must be viewed not only as a Customs problem
but also as one that is a feature of the business environment in Bangladesh This widespread tendency to evade payment of
taxes and providing false particulars is a major reason for inefficient practices such as the many checks and balances needed
before goods are cleared and released.
The problems faced by Bangladeshi Customs are numerous and complex. The situation is worsened by the fact that
soi-mie officers, particularly (it appears) those at Inspector levels, Clearing and Forwarding Agents and possibly other
unscrupulous importers are likely to sabotage any attempts to change the status quo from which they are openly benefiting.
On the other hand it was pleasing to note that senior officers such as Member (Customs), Mr. K. G. Sarwar, and Member
(Reform and Audit). Mr. S.I. Khan, were of the opinion that Customs reform was long overdue. Both officers believed that
the proposed Diagnostic Workshop under the CRM would provide a golden opportunity for officers to air their long held
views on how Bangladeshi Customs can develop in order to meet the clhallenges of the next century. They were also both of
the opinion that the CRM programme should be commenced sooner rather than later, a view that was strongly echoed by the
twfo consultants working on the computerization project underway (Messrs. A. R. Khan and Faiz Khan). The clear consensus
was that the diagnostic process should precede all further actions on existing projects so that any new actions plans can be
developed on the basis of the findings of the study.
At this stage it is possible to conclude that, at the Customs management level, the commitment to proceed with the
reform process has been adequately demonstrated. It has not, however, been easy in the time available, to determine with the
same degree of certainty whether, in view of the adverse cultural, economic, and possibly political environment, the Customs
administration is able to effectively carry through the substantial reforms necessary. It is not clear, for instance, what the
severity and effect of the anticipated anti-reform reactions will be. Despite these possible setbacks, however, it is considered
that the CRM program should be comimenced immediately. All action plans and recommendations for reforms should take
14 I. V. Mazarodze, Report on the World Customs Organization fWCO) Participation in the Wforld Bank AMission to
Bangladesh (1-14 July, 1998, Dhaka and Chittagong).



Project Appraisal Document                                                                           Page 60
Country: Bangladesh                                                           Project Title: Export Diversification Project
into account the peculiarities of the situation in Bangladesh and should include specific measures to counter these setbacks.
The Diagnostic Study Workshop will be an excellent opportunity to devise strategies to achieve this."
Impact and Process Indicators Proposed for the CMI Subproiect
(1) Impact evaluations
The most important part of the evaluation of the Customs Modernization Phase I subproject's effectiveness will be
assessments against indicators of the impact of subproject activities and accompanying reforms on service deliverv. Specific
benchmarks and expectations for FY2001 and 2002 in this regard will be developed by the Consultants and agreed with
NBRIIDA at Project mid-term review. They are expected to measure changes, compared with FY1999, in such variables as:
* Cost of complying with Customs procedures in terms of facilitation payments, sales lost or costs increased by clearance
delays, etc.;
* Cost-effectiveness of services provided, elapsed time for various key procedures, and number of disputes;
* Ratio of legal to illegal imports (methodology to be assisted by the PATC and PCU advisory teams);
* Client satisfaction, especially on the part of exporters.
These impacts are anticipated to result from both Component (1) on streamlining procedures under present structural
arrangements and Component (5) on piloting mofe fundamental departures from transactions-based assessment such as the
use of post-event audit and risk management techniques. It is recognized, of course, that much greater progress is likely to
occur beyond the Project's three-year horizon, when the piloted process changes may be implemented more extensively to
apply to all imports.
More specific performance indicators are set, as already stated in Annex 1, for the more narrowly defined
Components (2) on ASYCUDA and other MIS, (3) on Special Bonded Warehousing, and (4) on Duty Drawback, viz.:
* Y2k problems averted and ASYCUDA++ operational by December 2000;
* Satisfactory performance of other management information systems;
* New SBW monitoring system operational by June 2000;
* No SBW applicant too hard to monitor by June 2001;
* DD flat rates for 50% of major domestic inputs by June 2000;
* Cash Compensation scheme abolished (as all indirect export being well served by DEDO) by June 2001.
(2) Progress expectations for Component (5)
Component (5) activities will include the identification and diagnosis of organizational structures, work processes,
simplification, re-engineering work flow, and the design of other reforms that will be piloted within the project, so there is
time also to implement some of them more fully within the project horizon. The objectives will be accomplished through
cooperation among the Consultants, NBR management and staff, WCO and other advisors Subject to refinement by NBR
and the Consultants on the latters' appointment, specific activities and perfornance indicators could include:
